B003XWEQ1G|4.0|The critics deadpanned this addition to the Robin Hood film library, choosing to mostly focus on Crowe's \\"incorrect\\" accent and lament the sacking of the original script that saw the Sheriff as the hero and Robin as the villain. Reviews like that immediately set off alarm bells that perhaps these nitpickings were smoke screens. Afterall, who was alive in the late 12th century to tell us that the persona didn't have an Irish or Welsh flavoured accent? Why should the Robin Hood legend bow to 21st century story-telling conventions that grey villains and make heroes out to be the real misguided folk? (Although ironically the latter isn't far from today's truth.)<br /><br />Having seen and thoroughly enjoyed the version that was actually made (being this one), I understand why H-wood was disappointed. Crowe's 'Robin' isn't championing the redistribution of wealth and stands up for Life, Liberty, and the Pursuit of Happiness--the very ideals that would lead to the Magna Carta (the patriarch to America's own founding documents).The only other Robin Hood film to do this was 1938's [[ASIN:B0013N3DSE The Adventures of Robin Hood]] with Errol Flynn.<br /><br />Cate Blanchette brings as much gravitas to the Marian role as did Olivia de Havilland in '38. She's no easy mark for Robin and it takes the full length of the film for Robin to turn her eyes in a way that isn't a distrustful glare. I thought the touch at the end may have been a bit of a cow-tow to feminism and unrealistic to the period, but it was tasefully handled and in no way detracted from the story.<br /><br />My biggest gripe with the story was Scott's disappointing representation of the Crusades in film (again) and Richard's wars with the French. It's especially disappointing given the filmmaker's clear love for the long ago. I don't know.. maybe the studio forces such a period film to criticize the Crusades and be apologetic about Europe's role.<br /><br />The opening title card tells us that the film starts after King Richard has returned from his \\"plundering\\" during the Third Crusade and is making war on the French, who decided to take some of his lands while he was gone (under the duplitious watch of King Phillip, an ever present rival of Richard's throughout most of his life). In the case of the latter events--umm, isn't Richard simply being a good king by making war on those who would steal from him?<br /><br />But in the case of the Crusade, Scott lingers a bit longer, making sure that the audience knows that in his opinion, the killing of 2500 Muslims at Acre was \\"godless\\". The interesting thing here is that Scott would have us infer that the majority of those killed were women and children (quite the opposite in reality) and that he simply killed them for no reason (also untrue). In both cases, Scott stands at odds with most historians! But that isn't even the most important of the case.<br /><br />Within the context of the time, it is the concensus of scholars that Richard had little choice but to kill the prisoners. First, because he was leaving Acre and he couldn't have the men (who comprised the majority of the prisoners) joining up with Saladin to fight against him. Second, because Saladin had been stalling for time to regain his strength for an attack, and was playing games with an agreed upon prisoner exchange. Third, the prisoner exchange (which was to include the True Cross captured at Hattin) technically had been agreed to by a lesser-officer without Saladin's kowledge and it wasn't an agreement he particularly liked. And fourth, Richard may have wanted a bit of revenge for the hundreds of Templar and Hospitaller captives that Saladin had had beheaded (by amateur soldiers no less) following Hattin (see Scott's excellent [[ASIN:B000ICLRIE Kingdom of Heaven (Director's Cut)]]).<br /><br />The most interesting of all is that records, written by a known Muslim archivist of Saladin's army, speaks of the Acre incident very matter-of-factly.. as if he understoodthe decision and would have done likewise in a similar position of war. And later on, when Richard and Saladin come to terms at Jaffa, they deal with each other honorably--as if the Acre incident wasn't so much of an issue. This is significant given the long memory of the Muslim warrior.<br /><br />These departures from history must be known in order to appreciate just how good this film could have been had Scott made a film that brought these issues to the viewer.<br /><br />What the film doesn't depart from is a wonderful portrayal of the ideals of life, liberty, and the pursuit of happiness that would come from England's Magna Carta, and go on to influence the men who would bring democracy to the West centuries later; the film also remains true to the source material in that the story is NOT a commentary on class warfare (rob from rich to give to poor), that has burdened so many Robin Hood adaptations since Flynn's, but on the relationship of citizens and government.<br /><br />The audio and video of the blu-ray edition is fantastic to superb; the extras aren't bad, but there's no commentary from Scott, Crowe, and/or Blanchette?!<br /><br />Although the film bows the head a bit to modern filmmaking conventions (e.g. the Crusades were simply white Christian guys looting the world, killing \\"innocent\\" Muslims along the way, and robbing the Islamic community of its 'rightful lands'), it's refreshing to see a modern filmmaker get the Robin Hood legend closer to historical than any other. It was interesting to see a film that told a bit of a prequel than the story everyone mosly knows. Crowe's Robin Hood fits quite nicely beside my Errol Flynn version.osition of war. And later on, when Richard and Saladin come to terms at Jaffa, they deal with each other honorably--as if the Acre incident wasn't so much of an issue. This is significant given the long memory of the Muslim warrior.    These departures from history must be known in order to appreciate just how good this film could have been had Scott made a film that brought these issues to the viewer.     What the film doesn't depart from is a wonderful portrayal of the ideals of life, liberty, and the pursuit of happiness that would come from England's Magna Carta, and go on to influence the men who would bring democracy to the West centuries later; the film also remains true to the source material in that the story is NOT a commentary on class warfare (rob from rich to give to poor), that has burdened so many Robin Hood adaptations since Flynn's, but on the relationship of citizens and government.    The audio and video of the blu-ray edition is fantastic to superb; the extras aren't bad, but there's no commentary from Scott, Crowe, and/or Blanchette?!    Although the film bows the head a bit to modern filmmaking conventions (e.g. the Crusades were simply white Christian guys looting the world, killing \\"innocent\\" Muslims along the way, and robbing the Islamic community of its 'rightful lands'), it's refreshing to see a modern filmmaker get the Robin Hood legend closer to historical than any other. It was interesting to see a film that told a bit of a prequel than the story everyone mosly knows. Crowe's Robin Hood fits quite nicely beside my Errol Flynn version.

1439107955|5.0|The Emperor of All Maladies is a tremendous achievement.  It is the compelling story of cancer.  It is history, it is memoir, and it is a reminder that medical progress is the result of fits and starts and blind alleys and high hopes.  Overall, this book is an optimistic one.  Great strides have been made in fighting cancer, especially in the last 50-60 years.  Siddhartha Mukherjee documents the stories of the researchers whose single-minded determination has made it possible.    This book deserves the highest possibly praise.  It is even more remarkable because it is a first book.  Mukherjee could benefit from a more declarative prose style.  There is a lot of long winded verbiage at times.  Too many commas.  But the work is compelling because the story is compelling.  Mukherjee's injection of his own personal clinical history with cancer through his patients is worth a book on its own.    Mostly, Emperor of All Maladies is a glimpse into a closed world where death stares you in the face and even if you are a cancer survivor, you never look at life the same way again.  I am thankful that Siddhartha Mukherjee took on this task.  It is an accomplishment.

B0041KKYEM|3.0|It has taken me a while to see Darren Aronofsky's acclaimed 2010 psychological thriller probably because the marketing campaign at the time of its December release focused more on the film's sensationalistic, horror-film elements. In the meantime, Natalie Portman collected a truckload of awards for her searing portrayal of Nina Sayers, a talented ballerina whose mental state declines as pressure mounts around her starring performance in a new staging of Tchaikovsky's \\"Swan Lake\\" by a prestigious New York City ballet company. She is quite good, although I am less relatively enamored by the film itself, especially toward the end, which beyond recalling Aronofsky's earlier work, 2000's [[ASIN:B00005Q4CS Requiem for a Dream]], comes across as an intensely kinetic albeit derivative hybrid of [[ASIN:B003ICZW8M The Red Shoes]], [[ASIN:B00005K3NR Carrie]], and [[ASIN:0767802616 Single White Female]].<br /><br />Co-written by Mark Heyman, Andres Heinz, and John J. McLaughlin, the screenplay melds Nina's reality, dreams, and hallucinations into quite a ripe melodrama. Nina has long dreamed about playing the lead in \\"Swan Lake\\", and her fantasy comes true in a moment of pure bliss before her descent into madness. The ballet troupe's manipulative director Thomas Leroy picks Nina to replace aging prima ballerina Beth MacIntyre to play the Swan Queen, which is two roles in one - Odette, the virginal White Swan awaiting a kiss from the Prince, and her heartless twin, Odile, the sexually predatory Black Swan who lures the prince away. Leroy knows Nina is fully capable of playing the former but remains unconvinced she could exude the sensuality needed to play the latter. While she has trained her whole life for this opportunity, she remains an insecure, emotionally bruised woman still living with her overbearing mother Erica, a former ballerina who never reached the rarefied heights her daughter is about to enter.<br /><br />Complicating matters is Lily, a new arrival to the company from San Francisco and as liberated a free spirit as Nina is sexually repressed. Meanwhile, as Nina performs to the demands of the role, she crumbles in hidden ways that have her start imagining herself transforming into a swan. Present in almost every frame of the film, Portman is terrific conveying Nina's tentative nature in a way that makes her dedicated training all the more haunting for the sacrifices she makes to achieve her goal of artistic perfection. Within the confines of his predictable role, Vincent Cassel is malevolent enough as the autocratic Leroy, and Barbara Hershey really excels in her few scenes as Erica, showing why Nina's self-esteem has become practically nil. With her haughty manner and skeletal frame, Winona Ryder has more of an extended cameo as Beth, but she makes her fatalistic sense palpable. As Lily, Mila Kunis is almost effortless as Nina's saucy, distrustful competitor.<br /><br />Compounded by Matthew Labatique's constantly moving camera, Aronofsky's florid direction works on an almost surreal level for about the first three-quarters of the film's 108-minute running time, but his lurid horror-film approach gets excessive toward the end when Nina's confusion between reality and fantasy signals an inability to deal with the life she thought she wanted. The 2011 DVD/BluRay offers an extensive, 49-minute making-of documentary, \\"Black Swan Metamorphosis\\" which includes on-set footage and interviews with cast and crew. In addition, there are short featurettes focusing on the ballet sequences, the production design, the costumes, Portman's preparation for the role and Aronofsky's creative process. There are also interviews of the principals from Fox Movie Channel Presents, as well as the theatrical trailer.sco and as liberated a free spirit as Nina is sexually repressed. 0060936223|4.0|I was a great fan of Patti Smith in my younger days but as she has grown older she has become an example of the kind of New Age weirdness and political correctness that make my teeth grind.*<br /><br />So it was with some trepidation that I picked her memoirs of her time with Robert Mapplethorpe, expecting peace and love-type psychobabble.<br /><br />Instead, it was a well-written, disciplined account of her relationship with him and early life and mercifully free from her political opinions.<br /><br />She also resisted the temptation to get over-emotional about some episodes in her life, such as giving away her baby daughter when she was 19, and her description of how she and Mapplethorpe went their separate ways is refreshingly sober.<br /><br />Her obsession with French badboy poet Rimbaud becomes a bit tedious especially as she does not explain why he was so important to her.<br /><br />Nor does she give any good reason why she and Mapplethorpe chose to live in a cramped room in the Chelsea Hotel next to the room where Dylan Thomas died.<br /><br />The quality slips at times and name dropping abounds - Dylan (Bob, not the Welsh one), William Burroughs, Salvador Dali, Janis Joplin, Lou Reed and Alan Ginzburg** - but overall the book is low key, factual and fairly convincing.<br /><br />There are also incisive barbs such as her comment comparing Mapplethorpe with Andy Warhol: \\"I preferred an artist who transformed his time, not mirrored it.\\"<br /><br />It's difficult to believe she was as innocent and nave as she makes out.<br /><br />Patti Smith obviously regards herself as a \\"writer, performer and visual artist\\". Frankenstein does not arise from a table to act like a mindless zombie, Dr. Frankenstein does not scream \\"He's alive! This book, which at times seems more like a prequel to common Frankenstein movies, fluctuates about as much as the sea from which Dr. Frankenstein writes it. 0060936223|3.0|I'm not particularly familiar with Patti Smith's work, tho I know Robert Mapplethorpe's work a bit better.  I had high hopes for this book, as a memoir of a fascinating time in NYC, and of two artists growing together.  I wasn't entirely disappointed, I really enjoyed the memories of the Chelsea Hotel and Max's, and wasn't turned off by the name-dropping that a lot of people have commented on.  I also thought Smith did a good job conveying hers and Mapplethorpe's artistic world and approach to life-- making choices and having ambitions that truly set them apart from the rest of us who have regular jobs and less exciting creative lives than we'd like.  I was ok with the liberties it seems she may have taken at certain points in the story, as someone else wrote, most autobiographies have to be taken with a greain of salt.    My biggest disappointments began with Smith's inability to convey her angry punk persona or voice that she is known for.  She makes occasional reference to Mapplethorpe being concerend about her confrontational personality, and tells a story of Jan Wenner of Rolling Stone telling her he'll hire her even though she 'talks like a truck driver.'  Really?  I'd never know that.  She sounds like a French literature student throughout the book.  I'm a confrontational woman myself, and would've enjoyed seeing some of that on the page.  Along the same lines, I was surprised that she seemed extremely passive towards men, as one or two other reviewers have pointed out.  It's actually intriguing, although her total lack of introspection about her life meant that I had to draw all the contradictions between her supposed androgynous, angry powerful woman persona, and her poor romantic choices and seeming lack of agency in the development of her career.    Finally, this brings me to an undercurent of homophobia in the book that really surprised me.  Smith only refers to Mapplethope as a 'homosexual' never as 'gay' even though by the time she arrived in NYC 'gayliberation' was already a term.  At one point late in the book she refers to his 'sexual persuasion' as if it's a choice, or something that was not inherent to his being-- his art is synonymous with gay identity, and to use these terms shows a real lack of understanding or distancing from this essential aspect of his life.  I'd be interested to learn more about how she felt about Mapplethorpe coming out of the closet, she addresses it very lightly, even though in my mind it seems obvious that she remained in love with him probably until her marriage and move to Detroit.  She was happy to have sex with him long after he'd come out and they'd broken up-- not something most women I know would do if they really felt platonically about their former lover.  I beleive that part of her homophobia is her discomfort with her own sexuality.  As an LGBT woman I found so much of her story to be familiar to women who at some point in their lives have relationships with women.  Whether it is her androgynous physicality, her repeated relationships with gay men or otherwise unavailable men (married, on the road all the time), and her stated 'intellectual' interest in the styles of other women much girlier than herself, and her one line denial of being a  'latent homosexual', I think Smith may have some other issues to work out.  Maybe she has in real life, but it's disappointing she can't do it here, can't be reflective about her sexuality on the page.    Thank you Ms Smith for giving me something to write about-- a sign of a good book.

B000BVXC7Q|3.0|It would be a shame if someone coming new to Richard Strauss purchased this hefty 9 disc set and then would summarize this composer's orchestral work based solely on Kempe's recordings here. Since this set is now out of print, it's harder to acquire but I remember that it was promoted heavily on this site and in some critics' circles as the go to Strauss orchestral summary. There are some riches to be found on these CDs but also a lot of it is second rate compared to fiercer Strauss advocates such as Karajan, Reiner and Solti. I'll detail the contents of each disc and share my thoughts about where Kempe succeeds and where a listener might attain more insight from another maestro.     CD 1 - Here we have the two Horn Concertos, the Oboe Concerto and the Duet Concertino. Dennis Brain on EMI made a famous record of the Horn Concertos and that would be the primary competition. But since that is in mono sound, Peter Damm, the horn player for Kempe here, can rest easy. These are vivacious performances. I have never been very found of the Oboe Concerto and the Duet Concertino, so all I can say is good work since I've rarely listened to any other versions.     CD 2 - Some rather bizarre concerto pieces here to fill in the gap. The Burleske has been recorded frequently. Argerich has one on DVD, Gould also has one on CD. Peter Rosel, the pianist for Kempe acquits himself well but I would pick Byron Janis with Reiner as the one to beat. Moving on to the Parergon zur Sinfonia Domestica for piano left hand and orchestra. This is a concerto play on themes from the tone poem Sinfonia Domestica. The Parergon is not a major piece by any means and suffice to say, this is a fine account. Now to the Panathenaenzug for left hand piano and orchestra. Ridiculous title. The form of the piece is Symphonic Etudes in the form of a Passacaglia. I love passacaglia and variation form and this strange work is quite a delight. It is 27 minutes of blissful episodes. After a big opening statement, it descends into lovely, melodic, free-wheeling, almost jazzy variations. A different Strauss for sure. Rosel and Kempe keep the tempo lively and I was very pleased. For sure, 27 minutes is too much for such a piece but it's such a carefree delight, it might win over a number of listeners.     CD 3 - Here we start getting to some of the meatier works. Till Eulenspiegel is a romp that is not raucous enough in Kempe's good natured account. My favorite current version is Haitink but there are plenty of others. Next is Don Juan. Once again Kempe is firmly second rate without the polish, virtuosity or the heroic abandon needed for such a vibrant work. My preference is for Solti first and then Karajan second. Now onto the big show with Ein Heldenleben. Kempe underplays the music throughout. If you never heard any other conductor, you wouldn't know that this is fire breathing music that shook up the musical world upon it's premiere over a century ago. Karajan is the man for the occasion here without exception. He has three studio Heldenlebens and all put Kempe to shame. There is also Reiner, Solti, Rattle, Thielemann and Ormandy just to start with. All are leagues above the Kempe account.     CD 4 - The early Violin Concerto, dating from the years before Strauss became Strauss is good for a listen. But when we get to the grand Sinfonia Domestica, Kempe fails another major test. It's a warm, humane account but one that falls flat in comparison to the great Domesticas. Please go and find Karajan's miraculous Domestica on EMI. It is a stupendous feast of orchestral virtuosity, a tour-de-force performance that pits the Sinfonia Domestica as one of the greatest showpieces of the late romantic era. Furtwangler also has a great performance, so altogether Kempe can't compete with the best.     CD 5 - Zarathustra is up first. Kempe relinquishes all spectacle in favor of simplicity and good spirits. That might be enough for some. But if you want a blockbuster Zarathustra, it's Karajan again. Also try the famous Reiner from 1955, still in great sound. Up next is Death and Transfiguration, Kempe is easily bested by the likes of Karajan, James Levine and the unknown Tolga Kashif, all putting much more energy and intensity into this life and death tone poem. To round out the disc, Kempe made an arrangement of the famous waltzes from Der Rosenkavalier. This is a terrific selection and the Dresdeners play with style and gusto. But almost 18 minutes of waltzes can be too much of a good thing.     CD 6 - The Dance of the Seven Veils is sheer lighting under Solti and Reiner. Kempe cannot compete here. The Suite from Le Bourgeious Gentilhomme is music I avoid since it is a lot of mush over nothing. If one must have it, Reiner has the best account on disc. The excerpt from Schlagobers is Strauss at his creampuff worst. Then the suite from Josephslegende. Kempe is number one in this suite since it is recorded very rarely. The music is very superficial but contains some lush melodies.     CD 7 - The Metamorphosen for strings is Strauss at his most elevated. Too bad Kempe doesn't seize the moment here. For my money, no one has ever equalled Furtwangler and Karajan in this masterpiece. They are leagues above Kempe. The big show on disc 7 is the gigantic Alpine Symphony, this is cinematic music before that became a bad thing. Kempe humanizes the piece and plays it with the utmost dignity. This is one of his major successes in this set even though I still prefer the spectacular Herbert von Karajan version over all others.     CD 8 - The early Aus Italien symphonic fantasy gets a wonderful performance from Kempe and I would rank it among the best ever. Then Macbeth is also a winner, due mainly to the fact that this out of the way tone poem doesn't have much recorded competition.     CD 9 - Don Quixote is one of Strauss's greatest tone poems. Too bad it doesn't sound that way under Kempe, once again underplayed and lacking romantic ecstasy. One can easily turn tothe dream team of Rostropovich/Karajan on EMI to hear what this music is all about. Finally, the dance suite after Couperin is not a piece I've heard in any other capacity, so I just throw my hands in the air and say good enough.     To summarize, if you are looking for the best Strauss has to offer, you should investigate other conductors. Karajan does great things with Death and Transfiguration, Zarathustra, Ein Heldenleben, Don Quixote, the smashing Sinfonia Domestica and the Alpine Symphony. Reiner is great in Zarathustra and the Burleske. Solti awesome in Don Juan. Haitink superb in Till Eulenspiegel. Furtwangler is first choice in Metamorphosen and Karajan second.     Rudolf Kempe's major successes in this box set are these... an outstanding Aus Italien, a well done Macbeth, a ripe and humane Alpine Symphony, a delectable collection of waltzes from Rosenkavalier and the finest recording of the Josephslegende suite. One also can't forget the Panethezunaenzug symphonic etudes, which is just charming music to my ears. The sound quality throughout is good analogue stereo. Overall, this collection is too average in the great works to get a recommendation. Too many critics have steered listeners onto this set for reasons unknown to me. A lot of it is very mediocre. Great Strauss is easy to find, seek out my suggestions.

044656740X|3.0|This book is about financial literacy, a topic that is not taught in any formal school curriculum. Rich parents, the authors argue tend to teach those skills to their kids, whereas middle class/poor parents tend to stick to the old mantra of working harder for success. The key then is to buy assets that will continually strengthen one's income. As the author's best put it: \\"The rich by assets. The poor only have expenses. The middle class buys liabilities they think are assets.\\"<br /><br />Below are some of my favorite excerpts from the book:<br /><br />1) \\"Losers avoid failing. And failure turns losers into winners. Just remember the Alamo.\\"<br /><br />2) \\"Today, I often meet people who are too busy to take care of their wealth. Nobody has to tell them. Deep down they know. In fact, if you remind them, they often respond  with  with anger or irritation.\\"<br /><br />3)\\"In today's fast changing world, it's not so much what you know anymore that counts, because often what you know is old. It is how fast you learn. That skill is priceless. It's priceless in finding faster formulas-recipes, if you will, for making dough. Working hard for money is an old formula born in the day of the cave men.\\"<br /><br />4)\\" Many people only manage people they feel smarter than and they have power over, such as subordinates in a work situation. And that is financial intelligence.\\"<br /><br />An easy and insightful read into the area of personal finance. Very ambitious and theatrical in the vein of Hackett/Gabriel Genesis, the scope/breadth of this release is striking. The twin guitar sound we've grown to expect from Downing and Tipton is here and we definitely hear their respective styles; yet much of what they do in Nostradamus cannot help but remind one of Yngwie Malmsteen. There are some great acoustical moments and string arrangements here. When plugged in, the playing is fast (even thrash like at times), sometimes slow (one song is doom metal slow), soft and heavy. Thematically, one notices at once the similarity to Aphrodite's Child (the album 666),not to mention the sensibilities of Dante Alighieri, but that is where the similarity ends. Apocalypse, Exile, and Prophecy are all handled musically like no one else. Halford's voice is still amazing considering how long he's been at the helm. Like Downing and Tipton's guitars, his range is quite versatile. Early on in Act II, Halford sounds like David Byron (Uriah Heep)on one track singing really high/way up there. The production is excellent. This is a must own for any serious Judas Priest or progressive rock/concept album junkie like myself. While Judas Priest started out as a progressive rock band (if you consider about half of Rocka Rolla and much of Sad Wings of Destiny fit this category), this release is a far cry from those early recordings albeit very good ones. Nostradamus is a testament to a band not wanting to rest on its laurels, but one willing to try something new. They succeed at it very well.

B003UESJFG|5.0|I've seen this movie four times in two weeks. The last time I loved a movie enough to watch it that many times was in 1987, at the age of 13.  I'm 37 now, and much more critical about quality in movies than I was then, and I tell you now that Megamind is a brilliant film.    They Got It Right.  This movie incorporates the best elements that we've all seen before, remarkably few flaws, and turns out a hilarious, intelligent movie with a lot of heart. And kid-safe, too, although adults will get a lot more of the in-jokes.     First, the humor.  This movie is just plain fun to watch!  Just the right balance between silly-slapstick and witty irony, lots of hilarious one-liners, references to other sci-fi films and comics, and, rare for an animated film, tiny subtle shifts in body language that betray what a character is thinking without any words necessary.      Next, the characters.  All five of the main characters have a surprising amount of depth, making it easy to empathize with them, in part due to the gorgeous animation and incredibly realistic facial expressions.  Megamind in particular has very expressive eyebrows, and you can literally see wrinkles appear in his forehead when he's upset, or smile lines in his cheeks when he gives that irrepressibly gleeful grin of his...you can't help but love him.        The Story:  Megamind begins the movie as a mischievous, fun-loving, blue-skinned alien super-genius with a crush on pixie-haired reporter Roxanne Ritchi, (whose cameraman, Hal, also has his sights set on her) and a love of playing \\"the game\\" with the other (human-looking) resident alien, Metroman.  Metroman is not the typical Superman clone he appears to be at the beginning, either.        Having been raised in the city prison, Megamind is inexperienced, almost innocent, about many things in the outside world, which is one more reminder for us of his outsider status despite his being culturally of Earth. His only companion is his best friend Minion, a loyal fish sent with him to Earth who wears a robot gorilla suit.        The Twist:  When Megamind unexpectedly wins the \\"game\\", he is at a loss.  A surprise meeting with Roxanne while in disguise as a handsome museum curator abruptly changes the course of his life (and this movie!) Megamind experiences a phenomenal amount of character growth, a coming-of-age that is rife with hilarious zaniness and agonizing drama with a show-stopper finale ends on an incredibly satisfying note.     There are several extras you'll want to check out; all the standard stuff plus a sweet-sad deleted scene, an animated comic book that lets you see some of the past battles between Megamind and Metroman, and a 16-minute short adventure (Button of Doom, either included on the Blu-ray or make sure to get the DVD set that has it!), set after the movie that has Megamind hilariously battling one of his own inventions!        Gorgeous animation: The sheer amount of rich detail in this film make it worth watching many times. I highly recommend the Blu-ray, though, I watched a dvd version and you couldn't make out details like young Megamind's sheepish, sad-yet-hopeful smile in the class picture.  And unlike several other reviewers, I didn't notice any aliasing at all, and I looked for it.    All the characters have such tender attention to detail, including fingerprints, realistic freckles, and eyes that reflect different colors from the sources of light around them.  I'm not a fan of weapon design, but Megamind's De-gun (a ray gun that has multiple functions, including dehydrating animate objects instantly so they shrink into small cubes) has what looks like decorative bronze insets in graceful curves, truly lovely to behold.  The glass catwalks in the museum are beautiful, too.      Who won't care for Megamind?  In middle of the film is light on action and things blowing up, as Megamind learns to care for Roxanne while developing a plan centered around Hal, which could bore some die-hard action fansor young children.        I also appreciate the lessons taught: The choices you make control your destiny, not fate.  Intelligence will win out over brawn.  Prejudice is wrong and unfair.  Forgiveness.  Redemption.  When life gives you lemons, make lemonade...or giant robots!

0439023491|3.0|Warning, this review will contain major plot spoilers for both the second and third book.<br /><br />In all honesty, this book started out strong. You had immediate tension. In the first few pages we were finally introduced to the master villain who was only a mere shadow in the first book. And President Snow is as terrifying as he is repulsive, an excellent combination in a nemesis. Katniss is being punished for her impulsive actions of the first book, which have started a spark of rebellion in the other districts. Adding to the stress of a victory tour that she must take, which includes traveling to the districts of children that both Peeta and Katniss killed, Katniss must both pretend to be desperately in love with Peeta and make up for her use of the berries in the first games.<br /><br />The victory tour was intense. I was on an emotional rollercoaster ride while reading it. Collins handles the tension and raw emotional scenes beautifully. I found, more than once, my grip on the book tightening as I was fretting with Katniss over her family's safety and the general wellbeing of the rebelling districts. The victory tour was both hauntingly believable and yet equally and unimaginatively cruel. Just the idea of being paraded in front of the families and friends of fellow lost participants, participants that were in some cases killed by either one or the other of them, is unfathomable. And yet Collins handled herself masterfully. I really must sing her praises now, because I won't have anything to sing about in the next book.<br /><br />The one major grievance I had in the first half of this book was the needless love triangle. When handled well, love triangles can add complication to a story. When handled poorly it serves only as a distraction, a mere blind from the story itself and the things that actual matter. Though I understood Katniss' conflicted feelings for both Gale and Peeta, I did not understand Gale's. Peeta was very direct and honest about his feelings. Gale however only exhibited them when Katniss jumped out of his reach (which was an interesting delve into his character, don't get me wrong). I was actually under the impression that after they kissed and Katniss made no comment, that Gale had given her up. She didn't return the affections and it was a lost cause. Because I tell you what, if you kiss someone and they don't kiss you back, there is no greater turn off in the world- it's heart breaking. It's the \\"I love you\\" with a proceeding awkward silence. I would have expected Gale to start hating Katniss. But instead he pines after her, which is beyond my understanding.<br /><br />It's Jacob with Bella all over again.<br /><br />Finally the victory tour is over and it's one emotional bombshell drop after another. BOOM! She failed to stop the spark of her rebellious actions from catching and now her family is in grave danger. BOOM! Gale is whipped to nearly an inch of his life. BOOM! She has to go back and relive the Hunger Games all over. Wait what?<br /><br />Now I liked this and I will tell you why... It was completely unexpected. You thought that after Katniss finished the first Hunger Games that there was no way that she'd have to go back. It flipped your and her world upside down. It was an ultimate plot sucker punch.<br /><br />However, in the next book review, I will explain how Collins becomes a one trick pony. After you pull this off once, doing it a third time will not create the same effect. And in fact it will cheapen your story. But this isn't a review for the third book, this is the review for the second.<br /><br />Eventually Katniss composes herself and resolves to sacrifice her own life for Peeta's during this year's games. She conspires once again with Haymitch to make this happen. It's a noble cause. And we can see that Peeta has plans that are exactly the opposite. But they're both setting off into this year's games with a definite purpose.<br /><br />It's the games all over again. I won'tbore you with the unnecessary details. You can read the book yourself if you so wish. They meet new former champions. We get a brief look into Haymitch's own Hunger Games which to me... were disappointing. For such a heavy drinker, I was expecting there to be something in his games to drink about. But in all honesty, though his games were rough- I didn't think they merited the excessive drinking that he took to. He seemed a strong guy to begin with. I expected something deep and dark to be at the core of his drinking.<br /><br />For one of those special Quarter Quell games, Haymitch's was just overly disappointing. Just an average hunger games with more people dying. Visually this would have been horrifying to witness. But in written form, its just a bunch of nameless and faceless kids dying off screen.<br /><br />If I were Collins, I would have gone for a Of Mice and Men approach. Haymitch should have killed the girl from his own district. And that should have been what has been eating him up inside all these years. And this is how it should have gone down. The other districts should have had some real tyrants that year. Perhaps they were more bloodthirsty than normal. Maybe they enjoyed torturing the other tributes. Haymitch teamed up with the girl from his own district. When it comes down the line to the remaining tributes, his teammate ends up injured. Haymitch then realizes has to make a choice. Protect the girl at the expense of his own life. Let the girl get killed by the bloodthirsty tributes. Or kill her himself mercifully. Perhaps it even somewhat mirrors Katniss' own decision with the berries. But Haymitch decides to kill the girl before the other tributes can. He succeeds but it tears him up inside. Katniss could have watched this video with Peeta and been appalled by Haymitch's seemingly heinous act. While Peeta, on the other hand, could see why Haymitch did what he did and explain it to Katniss- how mercy comes in many forms.<br /><br />There's another reason why I would have introduced such a theme through Haymitch. Because this would have been a theme I'd carry on in handling either Katniss or Peeta's future fate. But since we aren't at that book yet, I'll continue this thought in the next review.<br /><br />But instead of this, we get some average recounting of a Hunger Games where the most Haymitch did was figure out how to use a force field to his advantage. In fact when Haymitch sees that they are watching his games, he doesn't even seem bothered. He seems almost proud- which annoyed me. And this is what he drinks to forget? Something he practically brags about? Here was a chance for us to really see a deeper side of Haymitch but instead all we get is a scrape along his superficial surface. Annoying. I thought Collins could have really handled that better.<br /><br />I do realize that in the last book it is revealed that the force field stunt got Haymitch's family killed. But I just felt that was a weak excuse of the author to explain his drinking. Why does he drink? Oh his entire family was killed by the bad guys. Never seen that before! Real original. It seemed a convenient excuse and just lazy on the author's part. If we never see Haymitch losing his family or never meet the family he loses, how can we sympathize with him? We are simply told that he lost his family. Well you and everyone else in this book, Haymitch. Suck it up. You're preaching to the choir.<br /><br />The interview was fantastic. Again it was intense bombshell after bombshell. Cinna's fantastic wedding dress design transforming into a mockingjay. And then Peeta's interview where he lied that Katniss was expecting a baby. All of these were fantastic twists of the plot. All of them captivating. And then the tributes standing in a line, united and holding each other's hands in the ensuing chaos. Beautifully done.<br /><br />But it was shortly after all this amazing work that things started slipping. And it all went south the instant Katniss got into the tube to head off into the arena. It is in that scene where Katniss watches helplessly as her friend Cinna is violently brained and then dragged out of the room that the dignity of the series is dragged out with him.<br /><br />Up until that point I was really enjoying the book. Sure it had its faults but what book is perfect? I was willing to forgive the faults for the awesome parts. I did not disagree with this scene per say. But this is the very last scene where I was okay with this book. I understood that Cinna had to die, on an author's standpoint. But I am never a fan of killing characters off screen. Especially main characters. Unless the character is not actually dead, to kill a character off screen is to leave the possibility that they are not actually dead. Because we weren't given a definite death scene, it leaves a character's fate up in the air.<br /><br />Collins is notorious for killing characters off screen and then mentioning in one brief offhand sentence that they are dead. Haymitch's family. The girls (I think one's name was Bonnie?) that were fleeing to the 13th district. Cinna. The red headed mutes that served Katniss and Peeta when they were tributes. Peeta's family. Not to mention most of District 12... Some of these off screen character deaths are forgivable. But several of them irked me. The fleeing girls for one. Why even introduce them if you, as an author, won't care to write out their fate. Even if in real life you never hear again from the cute flirtatious guy who served you coffee in Starbucks, does not mean that in fiction it is forgivable. In fiction, you have certain responsibilities to your reader that you need to fulfill. One of them is following through with characters you introduce. If you name them, you claim them. There's a line that I was told once in explanation to why things in real life do not always work in fiction, \\"An writer's job is always more difficult than God's.\\" What works in real life, has to be explained in fiction. You can't just go striking a person every few weeks by freak lightning and expect your readers to accept it. And you also can't just say offhandedly that main character's died safely out of sight and expect your readers to believe it.<br /><br />The instant Katniss is in the arena, she switches into survivor mode. Without hesitation, she goes back into the cold blooded killer of the first book. She doesn't even take a second to digest what happened to Cinna. She assures herself that now is not the time. Though I agreed with her, I didn't buy her attitude. I expected a few minutes of hesitation, tears, maybe even the beginnings of a break. Her best friend was just brained before her eyes. Even if she was about to be thrown into an arena to fight to the death, can she really just turn off emotions that quickly? By the third book, this doesn't seem the case. As she spends months in a practically catatonic state after her district is destroyed, when the rebels cry for her help. Then would be a good time to switch off her feelings too. But she decides she isn't ready and sulks for a good quarter of the book... But again, this is the second book. I must reign myself back from tearing up the third book just yet.<br /><br />And the former champions don't hesitate either. Even though they were united only hours before, they don't waste a single second in starting the blood fest. Katniss even thinks, `What did I think the handholding would last?' and I think, `Yes. It should have. Just a little bit please.' Now I'm not saying they all needed to throw down their weapons and refuse to fight. That would have been ideal. However if I wrote the story, I would have started with that. Especially with Katniss in her emotional fragility. They all hesitate. They simply refuse to move. Then the Capitol could step in and make things difficult, and have the games forced upon them. Even just a little hesitation would have been nice. Where they try to size up the other tributes and see whether or not they really were as united as they thought they were.<br /><br />But no, instead we jump right into the same scenario as the last book. Which is severely disappointing. That was a missed opportunity right there, Collins. Flew right by you, didn't it?<br /><br />For this part of the book, just go see Hunger Games Book 1. It's much of the same thing all over again. Except this time it's men and women dying not just poor defenseless children. Once again I must beg the question, why are these older men and women not seriously considering throwing down their weapons and refusing to fight? Later you find they had a secret agenda, or at least some of them did, but I have an even bigger problem with that secret agenda and I'll get to that in a moment.<br /><br />We get introduced to the force field again via flamb&eacute;ed Peeta. Pay attentions, kiddies! The force fields are important! Did you get that yet? And then we are treated to pages upon pages of Peeta and Katniss romance. It seems like Katniss has completely given up on Gale simply because he's not present at the moment. Attendance, Gale's Achilles' heel!<br /><br />Not only is this romance seemingly extremely decadent on the part of Katniss, but it gets a little painful in places. Katniss seems to be inflicted with a severe case of the Florence Nightingale effect, where she's only interested in a guy if he's bleeding, battered, or suffering from a near death experience. It's not just Gale that she's behaved this way towards. The only reason Peeta's received more attention is because he just happens to be in more near death situations than Gale has opportunity for.<br /><br />It's really disgusting if you think about it. No wonder she chooses Peeta, at the end of the book- he became clinically insane.<br /><br />The ending of Catching Fire climaxes upon a big reveal. Everyone was playing along so as to get the precious \\"mockingjay\\" to safety. Even the fellow tributes were sacrificing themselves for the better of the cause. They blow up the force field. And then the calvary comes to the rescue... to rescue the wrong people. Why they didn't just leave Katniss and rescue Peeta boggles my mind. For something they had planned so far in advance for, they were remarkably unorganized. Peeta should have been secured immediately as he was the best asset to their cause. And Katniss should have been left to die as a martyr. As cruel as that is, its the smartest course of action that the rebels could have taken. But even though it would have made more sense it would have messed up the direction of Collin's story so she couldn't have that happen. Katniss didn't have to die. She could have been held hostage much like Peeta had been. And we could have had two narrators for the last book... but unfortunately Collins had no foresight. And she dragged her story into the third book where she'd mercilessly shot it to death... WITH BEEEEEEEES! as my brother in law would so eloquently put it.<br /><br />By a writer's standpoint the ending of Catching Fire was rushed. In fact most of the time I was confused at what was happening. In a matter of pages we went from Katniss being attacked, to explosions, to Katniss thinking she had been captured and opting to kill Peeta out of mercy's sake (little late), to Katniss discovering that District 13 exists, to Katniss going psycho bitch on Haymitch's face. It was one huge revelation after another. I could kin it to \\"The Room\\" with the mother randomly announcing her real estate problems, relationship issues and having breast cancer all in one scene. I was severely tempted to throw spoons at this book as well. Though not as much as I wished to stake a spoon through the heart of the last book.<br /><br />When I had finally flipped the book closed at last, I felt disoriented by the wave of revelations made in the final few pages. It was a lot to take in. And Katniss' outburst was like a slap in the face. She obviously understood something in Haymitch's actions that I didn't. Had I been Katniss, I would have simply stood there numbly- trying to take it all in. But Katniss becomes the final explosion as she tries to claw a few more holes in Haymitch's face. For a girl who seemed to understand Haymitch's lies, even adding a few of her own to the batch, so as to pull the wool over Peeta's eyes-- surely overreacted once the tables were turned.<br /><br />In conclusion, I have problems with this book. But this book is clearly the lesser of the evils in the saga. Just wait until I start to dig into Mockingjay.lained in fiction. You can't just go striking a person every few weeks by freak lightning and expect your readers to accept it. And you also can't just say offhandedly that main character's died safely out of sight and expect your readers to believe it.<br /><br />The instant Katniss is in the arena, she switches into survivor mode. Without hesitation, she goes back into the cold blooded killer of the first book. She doesn't even take a second to digest what happened to Cinna. She assures herself that now is not the time. Though I agreed with her, I didn't buy her attitude. I expected a few minutes of hesitation, tears, maybe even the beginnings of a break. Her best friend was just brained before her eyes. Even if she was about to be thrown into an arena to fight to the death, can she really just turn off emotions that quickly? By the third book, this doesn't seem the case. As she spends months in a practically catatonic state after her district is destroyed, when the rebels cry for her help. Then would be a good time to switch off her feelings too. But she decides she isn't ready and sulks for a good quarter of the book... But again, this is the second book. I must reign myself back from tearing up the third book just yet.<br /><br />And the former champions don't hesitate either. Even though they were united only hours before, they don't waste a single second in starting the blood fest. Katniss even thinks, `What did I think the handholding would last?' and I think, `Yes. It should have. Just a little bit please.' Now I'm not saying they all needed to throw down their weapons and refuse to fight. That would have been ideal. However if I wrote the story, I would have started with that. Especially with Katniss in her emotional fragility. They all hesitate. They simply refuse to move. Then the Capitol could step in and make things difficult, and have the games forced upon them. Even just a little hesitation would have been nice. Where they try to size up the other tributes and see whether or not they really were as united as they thought they were.<br /><br />But no, instead we jump right into the same scenario as the last book. Which is severely disappointing. That was a missed opportunity right there, Collins. Flew right by you, didn't it?<br /><br />For this part of the book, just go see Hunger Games Book 1. It's much of the same thing all over again. Except this time it's men and women dying not just poor defenseless children. Once again I must beg the question, why are these older men and women not seriously considering throwing down their weapons and refusing to fight? Later you find they had a secret agenda, or at least some of them did, but I have an even bigger problem with that secret agenda and I'll get to that in a moment.<br /><br />We get introduced to the force field again via flamb&eacute;ed Peeta. Pay attentions, kiddies! The force fields are important! Did you get that yet? And then we are treated to pages upon pages of Peeta and Katniss romance. It seems like Katniss has completely given up on Gale simply because he's not present at the moment. Attendance, Gale's Achilles' heel!<br /><br />Not only is this romance seemingly extremely decadent on the part of Katniss, but it gets a little painful in places. Katniss seems to be inflicted with a severe case of the Florence Nightingale effect, where she's only interested in a guy if he's bleeding, battered, or suffering from a near death experience. It's not just Gale that she's behaved this way towards. The only reason Peeta's received more attention is because he just happens to be in more near death situations than Gale has opportunity for.<br /><br />It's really disgusting if you think about it. No wonder she chooses Peeta, at the end of the book- he became clinically insane.<br /><br />The ending of Catching Fire climaxes upon a big reveal. Everyone was playing along so as to get the precious \\"mockingjay\\" to safety. Even the fellow tributes were sacrificing themselves for the better of the cause. They blow up the force field. And then the calvary comes to the rescue... to rescue the wrong people. Why they didn't just leave Katniss and rescue Peeta boggles my mind. For something they had planned so far in advance for, they were remarkably unorganized. Peeta should have been secured immediately as he was the best asset to their cause. And Katniss should have been left to die as a martyr. As cruel as that is, its the smartest course of action that the rebels could have taken. But even though it would have made more sense it would have messed up the direction of Collin's story so she couldn't have that happen. Katniss didn't have to die. She could have been held hostage much like Peeta had been. And we could have had two narrators for the last book... but unfortunately Collins had no foresight. And she dragged her story into the third book where she'd mercilessly shot it to death... WITH BEEEEEEEES! as my brother in law would so eloquently put it.<br /><br />By a writer's standpoint the ending of Catching Fire was rushed. In fact most of the time I was confused at what was happening. In a matter of pages we went from Katniss being attacked, to explosions, to Katniss thinking she had been captured and opting to kill Peeta out of mercy's sake (little late), to Katniss discovering that District 13 exists, to Katniss going psycho bitch on Haymitch's face. It was one huge revelation after another. I could kin it to \\"The Room\\" with the mother randomly announcing her real estate problems, relationship issues and having breast cancer all in one scene. I was severely tempted to throw spoons at this book as well. Though not as much as I wished to stake a spoon through the heart of the last book.<br /><br />When I had finally flipped the book closed at last, I felt disoriented by the wave of revelations made in the final few pages. It was a lot to take in. And Katniss' outburst was like a slap in the face. She obviously understood something in Haymitch's actions that I didn't. Had I been Katniss, I would have simply stood there numbly- trying to take it all in. But Katniss becomes the final explosion as she tries to claw a few more holes in Haymitch's face. For a girl who seemed to understand Haymitch's lies, even adding a few of her own to the batch, so as to pull the wool over Peeta's eyes-- surely overreacted once the tables were turned.<br /><br />In conclusion, I have problems with this book. But this book is clearly the lesser of the evils in the saga. Just wait until I start to dig into Mockingjay.

Rockwell, in particular, comes off as an incompetent boob incapable of actually leading a company much less rivaling Stark. B000UZ4VC8|2.0|This review is 100% about the quality of the vinyl records (2 x 12-in 33.33 rpm 180g., sealed, new). I don't enjoy tics and pops on a new record but after 50 years of it I have abandoned any hope of vinyl perfection. However, I do not expect to pay $25 for one album and find it has a 1950's stereotypical \\"broken record\\" cartoon backtrack: a stuck record literally. As near as I can tell from many (too many)audiophile pressings there is no percentage in buying audiophile pressings: they cost twice as much but have the most serious quality problems at the same or higher frequency. Perfect flatness and reduced inner-groove distortion are nice, but they hardly matter if there are loud pops and tics and a backtrack.     I wish Knopfler had stuck with SACDs but respect his persisting with vinyl. The CD enclosed with the album is OK for a CD. The recording quality of the record is good to very good but not another Love Over Gold by any means. The pressing quality lowers the rating a lot. In between tie tics, pops, and skips, the surfaces are very quiet, except where they aren't.     As with any vinyl record, only buy from a dealer who will exchange it 100% of the time without a fee, and be prepared to return one out of five.

B00004S6GL|5.0|Elliott Smith exposed every part of himself through his music. He encompassed his life, even its most mangled aspects, in their entirety through song. The result was not the most easily perceived music, or even the most liked. It was beauty that wasn't obvious and thrown in your face; it's not immediately understood but it's deserving of the time it takes to divulge its meaning. The pain in the music is strikingly evident as it's part of his identity. But this is not to be critiqued.  Smith suffered the most horrible varieties of abuses, but channeled them into greatness. Elliott Smith was infinitely able contort even the most hideous hardships into accessible, admirable work. The result was universally relatable, raw emotion that could be interpreted as anyone wished. Because he didn't bitch and moan like James Blunt or make his cause blatantly obvious. He just lived for his music, and you can feel it when you listen to him.    Elliott Smith found idiosyncratic ways to make people feel. Whatever his music might evoke, it takes some self-interpretation to get there, making it more worthwhile. On Figure 8, you can't sit through \\"Wouldn't Mama Be Proud\\" and not be moved by the time he says \\"If I send you postcards from the side of the road/Photographs of moving parts about to implode/If I crawl to keep it together like you say you know I can do/To transmit the moment from me to you\\", if you truly are listening. It's beautiful and original, a rarity in modern music. Even his most simple songs like \\"Can't Make a Sound\\" are so expressive through the instrumentals alone that they resonate. And then there are the really abstract ones like \\"Son of Sam\\" that are catchy and make you think even if you have no idea what Elliott's talking about.      The one that sticks the most is \\"Happiness\\". With its simple lyrics, it's somehow tragically beautiful considering the circumstances. Looking back on it after his suicide, the lyrics \\"What I used to be will pass away and then you'llsee\\" are pretty haunting and saddening, but even extracted from the situation it's just another brilliant song. And it's disheartening to think that someone who could so inspirationally master the guitar, piano, drums, vocals, and even harmonica couldn't save himself from addiction and self-loathing in time. But perhaps it's that deep of a level of feeling that he was at, though disturbing, that it took to formulate such brilliant music. Two stabs to his own chest on October 21, 2003 and Elliott Smith was gone, but his music never will be.

0061689246|5.0|DISCLAIMER - I borrowed this book from a friend.  Neither of us knows Mr. Gaiman.<br /><br />I hate Fairies.<br /><br />If a book has the word \\"Fairy\\" any where in its description I tend not to be willing to read it.  God forbid its spelled \\"Faerie\\" or \\"Fae.\\"  That just REEKS of pretentia to me and I want nothing to do with it.  Why do I feel this way?  Well there are a number of reasons most of which probably herald back to the days of dating a certain young man who would take me to role-playing events as \\"dates\\" (yes, you know who you are, you read this blog and I blame you entirely :)).  But really it's because of the number of atrocious books out there on this topic.  It seems near impossible to find a book that isn't either insipid or basically Tolkein FanFic.<br /><br />But Stardust was written by Neil Gaiman.  Neil Gaiman wrote Sandman and Death.  Neil Gaiman: who summed up my adolescence in shockingly accurate and fanciful ways.  Oh if only my Sandman and I had knows we were meant to be brother and sister, so many things would have been easier!  Neil Gaiman is a genius.<br /><br />Stardust proved me wrong on every front.  Gaiman has written a fairytale; a true old fashioned fairytale that has high stakes and lofty moral lessons.  This is a fairytale with Faeries, Gnomes, mysterious parentage, witches and curses.  And I loved every single moment of it.<br /><br />There is not a single passage in Stardust that isn't expertly crafted or beautifully phrased.  Never over the top, the language chosen for this story suits the mood and tone perfectly.  Written in the style of cannon classics it took me a few tries to really get into the story.  I felt like it was smarter than me and I was working far too hard.  When I submitted to the simple story being told and allowed the words to flow over me and the tale wound itself around my heart, I was able to enjoy it.  I was able to enjoy not only the story but the experience of reading it.<br /><br />Stardust is an adult fairytale.  As you know I am constantly on the hunt for real adult books that challenge and stimulate my brain.  I like nothing less than a book that ends by pulling a rabbit out of a hat and calling it a surprise twist or a book that skimps over the necessary details just to cut it's raking to YA.  Stardust does none of this.  While there is sex in the book, it is neither racy nor inappropriate, it is essential to the story and Gaiman thankfully doesn't sacrifice the integrity of his story just to keep it PG.<br /><br />A few years back Stardust was made into a movie which while I enjoyed is not nearly as good as the book.  They changed a number of things, although I'm not sure why; the book as it is is plenty exciting and beautiful.  I highly recommend Stardust even if you have seen the movie.  The stories are the same enough that if you enjoyed the movie you will like this, but different enough it doesn't feel like a retelling.<br /><br />Gaiman has once again shocked me out of my expectations and proven that often I am wrong.  Stardust is a wonderful story and a beautiful piece of literature.  If you are interested in stories or writing or fairies or love or... well, honestly if you have a pulse you should give this book a try.fairytale. B004G5ZXVQ|4.0|To be a current REM fans means treating each new release as a comparison to the greatness -- or the occasional clunker -- of the past.  There is just no getting around it.    \\"Collapse Into Now\\" deserves to be judged on its own, but having been an REM fan since the mid-`80s, comparisons to the past remain inevitable.  Some of this collection recalls songs of the distant past and some of the recent past. The band continues to evolve, yet it stays true to its core sound -- that is, its two core sounds -- the jangling, acoustic-guitar strumming and plucked notes with relatively clear Stipe-ian vocals and the fuzzed-up electric-guitar-driven sound first heard on Monster.    \\"Collapse Into Now\\" contains a bit of both.  Many songs seemed to have flowed from \\"Accelerate,\\" which I thought was an excellent effort.      \\"Discoverer\\" is a great opener, with a bit of the \\"7 Chinese Brothers\\" guitar sound opening the song then shifting to a driving beat.  \\"All the Best\\" continues the theme, a punching beat.    \\"Uberlin\\" represents a switch back to the acoustic sound; it feels like it could have been found on \\"Automatic for the People.\\"  Peter Buck's trademark acoustic chord strumming combined with the plucking of the B and high E strings.  It really defines how he plays acoustically.  Listen to \\"Down by the Water\\" by the Decemberists and you will really hear what I mean.    I have to complain, though, that  \\"Oh My Heart\\" is way too close a sequel to \\"Houston\\" from Accelerate.  Michael almost re-uses the same line, going from \\"If the storm did not kill me, the government will\\" to \\"the storm didn't kill me, the government changed.\\"  I get the reference here, but something about it really bothered me -- to the extent that I am skipping this song.  Michael is a gifted songwriter, so why recycle your old work?  I know they recorded part of this in New Orleans, so this continues the Katrina theme, but it's too much for me.    I love \\"Alligator_Aviator...\\" but I long for Kate Pierson of the B-52s (as on \\"Shiny Happy People\\") on the female vocal party, though Peaches does her best to imitate the style.  \\"That Someone is You\\" is a perfect follow-up here, with Mike Mills adding great high-end harmonizing in addition to his usual stand-out bass lines.    \\"Me, Marlon Brando...\\" recalls Neil Young's Pocahontas (\\"Marlon Brando, Pocahontas and me\\").  I read that Michael said it is about him asking Neil for advice, though how you get that from the lyrics is beyond me.    \\"Blue\\" closes out the release in an unexpected way.  Michael's stream-of-consciousness lyrics lead the way through a trippy song, backed by Patti Smith's droning vocals  -- very reminiscent of \\"E-bow the Letter\\" on \\"New Adventures in Hi-Fi.\\"  It sounds like a therapy session.  I could have done without the flash back to \\"Disoverer\\" at the end, though.    All in all, well done by one of the best bands of the last 30 years.  Still not their best, but not \\"Around the Sun\\" or \\"Up\\" either.

B004L49K20|4.0|Please forgive me while I wax philosophic, but I thought I would give an honest, yet detailed review of the new album which purports itself to be the next \\"Rio\\"(I cringe to  hear the comparison to \\"Rio\\" because I consider it to be the definitive pop album of the '80's if not all time). To compare this album to \\"Rio\\", it is important to look at the construction of the record. \\"Rio\\" was largely dependent on the sound of the technology that was used to create the recording. This is what made it a cohesive and congruent collection of tunes. First and foremost, I'll start with the keyboard sounds which were central to the early \\"Duran\\" sound. Nick Rhodes used the Roland Jupiter 8 synced to a Roland Compurhythm CR-78 to create the textures to almost all of the songs on the album. The Jupiter's random arpeggiator can be found in almost all of the songs. Rhodes also used the Prophet 5 synthesizer for the sound effects including helicopter and wind type sweeps. For leads, He used a Crumar Performer string synth, often run through a Roland SPH-323 Phase Shifter which gave that unique washing sound. He ran an output of the CR-78 to Roger who used it as a click track. Roger also used the technologically distinct sound of Simmons pads and LinnDrum samples. John used a single-pickup Aria Pro II Super Bass run through an Ampeg SVT tube amp, which if drive, gives a very warm sound. He adds chorus for \\"Lonely In Your Nightmare\\". Andy uses a Les Paul run through a (speculation here) Roland Jazz Chorus amp to give his leads a very \\"synthesized\\" sound. Simon constructed the lyrics to the record much differently to the process he now uses. He called them in an interview \\"Fantastical\\", because he lacked the life experience to write any other way. Actually, Simon was right in line with many other artists at the time who wrote similarly. It was a very poetic time.  So...is AYNIN the new \\"Rio\\"? Nick uses Roland synths (VSYNTH) and arpeggiators with CR-78 samples - Especially in \\"The Man..\\".but not to the extent of \\"Rio\\". John no longer plays the bass aggressively, melodically, in the style of the late Bernard Edwards(well kind of on \\"Girl Panic). Roger holds it down, but the drums are overall less electronic. Ironically, a couple of points remind me of \\"modern English\\" and another couple of Gary Numan. Simon's lyrics are not to the level of \\"Rio\\", but his singing is excellent nevertheless.   \\"Rio\\" it is not, but it is the best they have done in years and I would rate it third behind their first two albums (\\"Rio\\" first of course). Ask yourself two questions: Would you want another \\"Rio\\"? How many chances will \\"Duran\\" have to \\"Come Back\\"?(Wedding Album, Astronaut etc...). With the exception of \\"Medazzaland\\", it is the best work they have put out in almost three decades. I just hope that all Duran needs is \\"now\\".

159420229X|5.0|\\"Moonwalking with Einstein\\", by Joshua Foer (a freelance journalist), places the `spotlight' on the question as to how closely one's `memory' is associated to one's `identity' by recounting the author's experience developing his own power of recall (which, by Foer's own admission was average, at best), and ultimately winning the 2006 U. S. Memory Championship. Throughout his `mnemonics journey' Foer explores the related questions as to whether improving his memory would enable him to become smarter/wiser, more persuasive/attentive/self-confident, and a better journalist/friend/boyfriend.     Foer's adventure starts out with him wondering how the `smartest person in the world' would stack up against the `strongest person in the world'. However, after conducting numerous Google searches Foer discovers that it is much more difficult to quantify `brains' than `brawn'. So Foer `settles' for the person who ostensibly has the `best' memory, a `freakish genius' named Ben Pridmore who is the reigning world memory champion. Foer is inspired by Pridmore's earlier statements in a newspaper that anyone is capable of performing his memory feats by learning and applying the proper `technique' and understanding how memory works. As an observer at the 2005 U. S. Memory Championship Foer meets a young grandmaster named Ed Cooke, as well as other `mental athletes'; all of them reinforce Pridmore's assertion that anyone with proper training could perform the memory feats they do. Accordingly, Foer is inspired to embark on an intense training program (lasting one year) with the goal of competing in the U. S. Memory Championship. Ed Cooke, in effect, becomes Foer's personal `yogi and manager'. Ed explains the importance of memory among the ancient civilizations, and laments the reality that memorization is becoming a lost art; i.e., society's `internal memory' continues to erode as we increasingly depend on `external memory' (e.g., computer, cell phones, paper, books, et al).     Foer learns about a 30-year study done (1928-1958) by A. R. Luria of a man called \\"S\\" who remembered everything to which he was exposed, and whose memory never degraded. As it turns out, \\"S\\" is afflicted with Synthesia wherein a stimulus of one sense (e.g., hearing a sound) is perceived as a sensation of another sense (e.g., seeing a color); this malady actually facilitated his extraordinary memory. Foer states, \\"S could memorize complex mathematical formulas without knowing any math, Italian poetry without speaking Italian, and even phrases of gobbledygook.\\" Luria eventually wrote a book about \\"S\\" titled, \\"The Mind of a Mnemonist: A Little Book About a Vast Memory\\".     Foer meets a group of students from the Samuel Gompers Vocational High School (competing in the 2005 U. S. Memory Championship) and their American history teacher, Raemon Matthews who expresses his firm believe that memory training should be an integral part of the formal educational system. Later, while watching a TV program Foer learns about a 26 year-old British `prodigious savant' named Daniel Tammet who displayed genius in numerous different areas, e.g., complex multiplication and division in his head, identifying prime numbers up to ten thousand, speaking more than six languages, inventing his own language, et al. Foer eventually meets Daniel Tammet. (Foer indicates that two disabilities have conspired to produce Tammet's amazing abilities: synesthesia, and Asperger's syndrome.). Foer also meets Kim Peak (aka Rain Man), the prodigious savant featured in the movie titled, \\"Rain Man\\".  As the antithesis of people with extraordinary memories, Foer meets `EP', a man whose memory is limited to events prior to 1950, plus those events to which he has been exposed during the most recent few minutes; EP virtually lives in the present.      This book is not a cookbook on mneumonic techniques, although it does provide an overview of some techniques, e.g.,   (1) Memory Palace - This technique, which was inventedby Simonides of Ceos, is also known as `journey method', or `method of loci'. It can be used for, e.g., memorizing a shopping list, a string of random digits, prose, poetry, et al.   (2) Major System - This technique, which is less powerful than the Memory Palace, is a simple code whereby numbers are converted into phonetic sounds which, in turn, are converted into images to be used as a memory palace. The Major System approach was invented by Johann Winkelmann.  This book provides a distinction between `natural memory' (which Foer calls \\"the hardware you're born with\\") and `artificial memory' (which Foer calls \\"the software that runs on your hardware).      I found this book to be enlightening and thoroughly engaging. It provides deep insight into the history of the `art of memory' and the close association between one's memory and one's identify. Foer provides strong motivation for developing one's memory via mnemonics training.

B004EPYZPI|5.0|\\"The Lincoln Lawyer\\" with more twists and turns than a corkscrew reminded me of those classic L.A. crime thrillers like \\"Chinatown,\\" and the hardboiled school of Dashiell Hammett, Raymond Chandler, and James M. 0061743526|5.0|This is a novel that I have read countless times over the years and it never fails to connect with me on some level with every reading. That is no small feat for a book to accomplish. To speak to people the world over, for over 50 years, means that there is something very universal in this text.<br />We are all the mockingbirds of the title, and anyone who has reached the age of majority knows the feelings that the loss of innocence, and the harsh light of reality can create in a person. Harper Lee wrote a novel that captured the essence of that most universal of experiences, and I for one am moved by it every time.<br />I won't rehash plot points, but I will give you some reasons why you should read, and then reread, this text.<br />The first reason is for the beautiful depiction of imaginative childhood. The narrator, her brother Jem, and their friend Dill embody most of the traits of childhood, and their creative games, and thought processes are a joy to behold. The voice of Scout (the narrator) is a pitch perfect recreation of childhood and childish impulses.<br />Another reason is a pretty simple one actually. The character of Atticus Finch is one of the noblest literary creations ever written. I want to be like him, and there are not many characters in literature I can, or would, say that about. The fact that being like Atticus is possible makes him even more endearing to me.  He is someone who is noble in every sense of the word, and serves as an inspiration for so many reasons. He is a good father, a decent and empathetic man, and a person who tries to see the good in almost anything. The fact that there are so few Atticus Finches in real life makes him seem all the more desirable to me. It is very obvious as you read this text that Ms. Lee loves this character.<br />There are numerous other reasons why this is a stellar work of genius, not least of which is the wonderful plotting of the novel, the excellent and fleshed out supporting characters (these people are real to me) and the message that Lee find numerous ways to reiterate throughout the book.<br />Ms. Lee has created a pastoral version of the Depression era south, and even though there is vicious bigotry and hatred depicted in the novel, Ms. Lee (through her characters) does not give up on them, or us. We and the world can be better. We just need to remind ourselves to walk in other people's shoes once in a while.nd the message that Lee find numerous ways to reiterate throughout the book.  Ms. Lee has created a pastoral version of the Depression era south, and even though there is vicious bigotry and hatred depicted in the novel, Ms. Lee (through her characters) does not give up on them, or us. We and the world can be better. We just need to remind ourselves to walk in other people's shoes once in a while.
