B003Y5H53S|5.0|Rango is a very unique animated film that I honestly don't think I can evaluate by traditional animation standards.  Yes, it is animated (a good first outing for special effect giant Industrial Light And Magic, for whom this is their first fully animated film) and yes, there is lots of slapstick material for the family audience, but the true intended audience of Rango is clearly more seasoned moviegoers.  This is an animated film I think will be most appreciated by those who are familiar with the many films it draws inspiration from. Director Gore Verbinski switches gears from his Pirates of the Caribbean trilogy, which had gotten pretty over-the-top by At World's End to deliver a light, enjoyable animated feature.<br /><br />Johnny Depp is the titular \\"Rango\\" - or, at least, that's who he claims to be.  You see, the chameleon was a family pet with an overactive imagination who dreamed of writing plays and acting out heroic characters, but whose true name the audience is never given (no doubt a nod to the Man with No Name western trilogy starring Clint Eastwood).  When he gets lost due to a freak accident in the Mojave Desert his travels take him to the small animal community of Dirt he quickly forges a false identity under the name \\"Rango\\"- a rough, gun blazing icon who once killed seven men with a single bullet!  By chance he also manages to kill the hawk (which happens to sport a steel tipped beak) which only raises his prestige in the town, resulting in his becoming the new town sheriff.<br /><br />Johnny Depp gives a very enjoyable performance as Rango, but I don't think is as \\"likable\\" as the script seems to want him to be- or I should say: he isn't likable in the way a \\"family movie\\" hero should be likable.  He is often cowardly, self-centered, as well as a compulsive liar whose imagination runs rampant.  One scene in particular stands out for Rango's \\"unlikable\\" factor, when the citizens of Dirt are rioting over the water shortage and Rango (who has been thirsty most of the movie) chooses to drink four cups of water while lecturing the citizens on how they can't all get water or else they'd run out.  Naturally he looks like a perfect ass for this entire segment that can't really be sympathized with by the audience.  This is fun for older audiences who like a more flawed protagonist, but at times the script tries too hard to make him seem like a charming, upstanding role-model, when lots of the time he just comes across as a jerk.<br /><br />Most important to know about before seeing Rango is undoubtedly Sergio Leone's Italian made Westerns (Or \\"Spaghetti Westerns\\" as we lovingly tend to call them), particularly the ones featuring Clint Eastwood in his break out role as The Man with No Name.  The music, the look, the atmosphere, and the classic western stand-offs are all in loving tribute of Leone's heavily stylized portrayal of the west.  Aside from the pure Leone Western clich&eacute;s and archetypes the primary plot of Rango surprisingly is taken from Roman Polanski's detective mystery Chinatown.  In both the plot revolves around a city (the small desert community of \\"Dirt\\" replaces L.A.) that is experiencing a severe draught, yet someone is dumping fresh, usable water into the desert, away from the eyes of citizens for some unknown reason.  If you have seen \\"Chinatown\\", Rango makes no attempt of hiding who the antagonist will turn-out to be, as the villain dresses the same as the villain of Chinatown, and has the EXACT same plan involving water and real-estate, but that isn't really bothersome because Rango puts a lot of heart in its tributes to classic films.  They know that they're using Chinatown's plot and I don't think they try to sell this film's plot as being anything other than an excuse to pay tribute to the classics, so this isn't a problem,<br /><br />The secondary antagonist of the film is the one who's already been revealed via trailers: A leather hat wearing serpent by the name of Rattlesnake Jake.  Coming out with a tough as nails voice and a segmented Gatling gun for a rattle this is honestly perhaps the most awesome villain I've seen in an animated film in years!  He even tells a character that he comes from Hell (oh yeah- this film does take a risk for an animated film of using lots of \\"Damns\\" and \\"Go to Hell!\\" type talk that parents wouldn't want repeated by their children).  With all the other sentient animals in this film (it will always bug me why some animals in here are anthromorphic and sentient while others, like the roadrunners, are not...) it is really impressive to see how the folk from Industrial Light And Magic were able to bring credibility to a snake being capable of doing the same things as all the other critters, even with the lack of hands.<br /><br />Jake is voiced amazingly well by British actor Bill Nighy, who, like Depp, previously worked with director Gore Verbinski in the Pirates of the Caribbean films, portraying tentacle-faced ship captain Davy Jones.  He really must have had fun doing this outlaw of a Diamondback Rattlesnake (who is so villainous he even has a little moustache pattern on the scales above his mouth!). I'm serious, this villain is just pure fun to watch.  He is every-single awesome outlaw stereotype wrapped up in a delicious, slivering package!  He has a foot (tail?) in for being a likely pick if I choose to compile a list of my favorite villains for the 2011 movie season.<br /><br />Gore Verbenski and composer Hans Zimmer made it apparent that they had a mutual love of Sergio Leone and Ennio Morricone's musical scores.  Once more Zimmer reflects on the themes of \\"The Good, The Bad, And The Ugly\\" and \\"Once Upon A Time In The West\\" with extraordinary effectiveness.<br /><br />Rango is not a perfect animated movie, nor do I think young children will appreciate it as much as viewers in their upper teens and older.  It won't traumatize any kids, but it probably is far too intense for them, and the best of the film's humor they'll likely have no hope in understanding due to not being exposed to the countless films referenced- many of which are rated R films from the 1960s-1970s.  There's Chinatown and the Leone Spaghetti Westerns, but other films I saw referenced include: Apocalypse Now, Star Wars, The Wizard of Oz, any given zombie movie, and I'm sure there were many more that have simply slipped my mind.<br /><br />As a standalone film I do think Rango was mighty entertaining for what it was- a loving tribute to the western genre and other classic films.voice and a segmented Gatling gun for a rattle this is honestly perhaps the most awesome villain I've seen in an animated film in years!  He even tells a character that he comes from Hell (oh yeah- this film does take a risk for an animated film of using lots of \\"Damns\\" and \\"Go to Hell!\\" type talk that parents wouldn't want repeated by their children). 0316010669|4.0|Blink: The Power of Thinking Without Thinking by Malcolm Gladwell interestingly enough is a book that will make you think about thinking. The idea that most of our decisions are made in the blink of an eye and that we can't even explain the reasoning behind them is intriguing and sometimes a little scary. Readers may find themselves pausing while going about their everyday activities to question a decision they made without realizing they made it. Gladwell describes the concept in a way that leaves the reader wondering why they never thought of the points he makes. His examples give a deeper understanding of the idea of making a decision without knowing why even if the examples are sometimes experiments that make the reader squirm a little as they read. The section where Gladwell discusses a military exercise gives a moment's pause about the inflexibility of military thinking but could also be applied to any situation that pits two groups against one another. Gladwell also spends quite a bit of time talking about the results of testing that examine our preconceived and possibly subconscious thoughts about others. He explains the benefits and the pitfalls of thinking without thinking. Blink is a simple but powerful and fascinating look into the way our brain works often without our knowledge to make split-second decisions.

0375842209|5.0|The novel The Book Thief was the first book I had read by Markus Zusak but from the very first page I was captivated. It is narrated by Death, who shares the story of a young girl named Liesel living in Nazi Germany. She is orphaned from a young age and all she has left to remember her family by is a book she stole while at her brother's funeral. It's not until her foster family takes her in and she begins to learn to read that she develops an avid love for reading. She begins to steal books at which point Death gives her the nickname \\"The Book Thief\\". The plot thickens further as Liesel's foster family ends up hiding a Jew in their basement.    The main reason this book is so unique and captivating is the style in which Zusak writes. He describes simple things in beautiful, poetic ways that stretch your imagination and makes you ponder if your perception of things is really the correct view. He crafts sentences with grace and flow that other writing seems bland and lacking. For example, he describes a red sky like \\"soup, boiling and stirring. In some places, it was burned. There were black crumbs, and pepper, streaked across the redness.\\" He also manages to make his narrator, Death, poetic and relatable. \\"A human doesn't have a heart like mine. The human heart is a line, whereas my own is a circle, and I have the endless ability to be in the right place at the right time. The consequence of this is that I'm always finding humans at their best and worst. I see their ugly and their beauty, and I wonder how the same thing can be both.\\" Even though this book is fun of action and drama it still has the power to make you stop and think about the simple phrases Zusak uses to make his point.     This book is a gem, a rare find that come only once in a great while. It's poetic beauty in describing Nazi Germany and the people living there is unique and chilling. It gives you a new look on World War II and the effects it had on the people living in Germany.  It made me laughand cry and truly care about the characters. The Book Thief is one-of-a-kind and you'll want to read it again and again.

How wrong I was...     The series this time moves to New York, and the main plot concerns a peace treaty between the US, Russia and a fictional Arab nation. Terrorists are determined to halt the treaty by assassinating the Arab President (a superb performance by Bollywood actor Anil Kapoor) before it is signed. Of course, there are the usual twists and turns (though -- thankfully -- nowhere near as many as in season 7), and inevitably the now-retired Jack is brought reluctantly back into the front line to save the day.     This isn't so much as a new 24, but a return to the old style. The tension builds slowly as the clock ticks; there is much more emphasis (like the old days) on creating tension than all out drama. Sure, (naturally) lots of dramatic things happen, but it just felt much more balanced, and less OTT than it had become -- like it used to be. That said, though, when things do get hot then it is as explosive and intense as ever. No spoilers, but the last 4 episodes in particular are as good as any season. It's up there with 5 for a breathtaking finale!...     The acting, again, is first class (especially the Arabian presidential family, and again Cherry Jones returning as President Taylor). Kim is also in New York (but doesn't need rescuing this time around!) and Chloe is still struggling to sort out the muddles at CTU! Freddie Prinze Jr. is solid as the new lead CTU field agent Cole Cortez (a little similar to Chase in Season 3). There are a couple of goofy side plots (as always), but they don't detract from the strong central stories which develop (and nothing as bad as Kim's babysitting adventure!...)Personally, my only quibble is (and it's more about the series as a whole than season 8) that I think they shouldn't have overused Nina Meyers in seasons 2 and 3. I think it would have been the perfect ending to the series if they had saved her showdown with Jack till the last act, bringing it full circle to a more natural conclusion. Other than that, this is one of my favourite series, and season 8 one of my favourite 24 seasons. I can't really separate it from 1 and 5 -- they are all brilliant in different ways. 3 was next best then 6, 2, 4 and 7. That's only my opinion, though!...     I'm sad that it has finished, but I'm glad it found it's form again before bowing out. Hopefully the film that is planned will be made... I think the main reason this works (as others have mentioned -- as well as the excellent direction by Fincher) is that Zuckerberg (and most of his peers) are portrayed as being just as irresponsible and foolish as you expect them to be! This isn't the story of how a genius founded a global empire, but of how someone with little social skills managed (through a combination of hard work, other peoples ideas (largely stolen) and sheer luck) managed to revolutionise the Social Networking phenomenon. It portrays him as a dedicated professional and programming genius, but with little charisma or morality. If it had tried to idolise him, then it would probably have lost me early on; as I say, after the first 30 minutes, I was totally riveted. 0756405890|4.0|This book is the story of a humble bartender, actually a legendary hero, narrating his tale to a collector of truths.  What makes it interesting is that we first hear the \\"bard's tale\\" version of the man (Kvothe the Dragon-Slayer, Kvothe the Bloodless, etc.), and then, as the tale unfolds, we learn what _really_ happened--which often has Kvothe succeeding only by luck, trickery and wit.    I liked Rothfuss' concept of exploring the difference between Kvothe the legend and Kvothe the orphaned child/awkward teenager--which is what he is throughout the first book.  Despite being extraordinarily gifted at magic and music, he struggles throughout the novel:  dead parents, homelessness, a perpetual lack of money, and a complete and utter lack of confidence with women.  Some reviewers have described Rothfuss (the author himself, not just the character) as being \\"obsessed\\" with money.  Yes, the issue of money does come up a lot in young Kvothe's life, especially while he is at the University, but this is obviously to show how his poverty was a constant problem that he had to overcome (while many of the other students didn't even have to think about it).      I found Kvothe's character, overall, to be very sympathetic.  He is extremely gifted, but his talents often get him into more trouble than they get him out of.  He can be arrogant at times, but given his circumstances, I found his behavior understandable and not generally off-putting.  I think those reviewers who complained that he was \\"too awesome\\" kind of missed the point of the book, which was that there is often a big difference between the legends and the truth!      In terms of writing style, I thought the prose was very smooth--it's not the first thing that jumped to mind when reviewing the book, but it's WAY better than a lot of fantasy books I've read.  I can't quite understand the reviews that say the prose is horrible.  I'm not exactly sure what these reviewers are looking for.  Sure, you could probably findsome lines in the book, which taken out of context might sound a little awkward, but it seems awfully nit-picky.      For all its good points, I must admit that I wasn't as blown-away by the book as I'd hoped.  It came highly recommended to me and perhaps my expectations were somewhat lofty.  The story does get slow for lengthy sections of the book.  I can understand why some people wouldn't care for the emphasis on Kvothe's money problems--maybe for a fantasy book, this hits too close to home?     Also, I found the secondary characters in the book to be weak.  Kvothe's friends at the University are utterly forgettable.  His love interest, Denna, doesn't quite seem special enough to me to warrant his obsession with her.  There is one memorable scene where she sings a duet with him, which made me think she would do more with her music, but later her talents always seem secondary to his (of course, it's told from his point of view, so that may be why!)  I hope her character is developed more fully in the next installment of the series.     All in all, I would definitely recommend this book to people who like intelligently written, character-driven fantasy.  I'm looking forward to reading the next book.

I love how the majority of the cast has the characters retain the same name as the actors who play them. As I was saying the couple's interactions for the most part are completely realistic (though Micah's insistence at about the half way mark of the movie to still figure it all out on his own get's a bit tiresome.) People who don't believe in ghosts aren't going to believe that a person can be haunted and try to poke at it to come up with a rational answer which is exactly what Micah does. Katie who knows she is haunted just wants to leave it be as provoking it always makes it worse.<br /><br />The movie has a very slow build up which can hurt it with audiences who have no patience in their movies though the buildup brings up quite a few mind blowing moments of suspense for those who do like a slow start. They are able to capture exactly what makes our cast so afraid of going to bed at night. It's an amazing achievement to be able to get a crowd react so much to purely suspense and not scares in a film and something that is mainly a lost art in Hollywood. Too many people enjoy instant payoffs so the slow buildup has been overlooked for years - Paranormal Activity shows that this is still a valid art form in the realm of making horror.out on his own get's a bit tiresome.) B001MYIXAC|1.0|*** This review may contain spoilers ***<br /><br />It doesn't astound me at all that the majority of mainstream film critics gave \\"Let the Right One In\\" a free pass! They have been suckered in by so many pretentious 'art' films these days, why can't an ordinary horror film be included in their pantheon? The critics' level of self-deception is so high on this film that there were zero negative reviews, only one mixed and 29 positive reviews on Metacritic (the mixed one was by Owen Gleiberman of Entertainment Weekly; unfortunately, it's only a capsule blurb).<br /><br />For the majority of internet posters with dissenting opinions, their main objection was that the film was too slow-paced to be worthwhile. There are plenty of films that are slow-paced but they still hit the mark, so to criticize the film merely on the grounds that it's tedious, misses the point as to why 'The Right One' is so reprehensible.<br /><br />It begins in a Stockholm suburb and it looks like this is going to be another tired serial killer retread. The killer (Hakan) has slit the throat of a young man walking in a park, hangs him upside down, dangling from a tree, and then attempts to collect the blood into a gasoline can as it drips from the victim's neck. Soon, however, we learn that Hakan is actually a vampire (or rather an assistant to a vampire who appears to be 12 years old). The 12 year old 'female', named Eli, actually has been alive since the middle ages (the book that the film is based on includes more graphic details on Eli's origins--that she, rather he, was castrated by a sadistic vampire nobleman).<br /><br />Some critics (such as Roger Ebert) became enamored with the relationship angle between Oskar, the lonely 12 year old protagonist, and his new found vampire friend. Oskar already has a fascination with crime to begin with, cutting out articles from the local newspapers detailing sordid murders occurring in nearby localities. While Oskar seems to have a nose for crime, the local police in 'Let the Right One In\\" seem to be oddly absent during the investigation into the killings initiated by Hakan and his juvenile master, Eli.<br /><br />One wonders why Eli suddenly takes a liking to little Oskar since she has no guilt feelings about draining the blood out of various other upstanding members of the community. Since those community members all seem to depicted as a bunch of louts or buffoons, I guess it's okay that Hakan and Eli can do whatever they want to them with impunity. Eli further displays her 'sensitive side' when she advises Oskar to strike back at the local bullies who have been harassing him.<br /><br />There are very few films that will feature a murderous monster who is depicted as not being so bad after all. Somehow it doesn't bother Oskar that his gal pal goes around dispatching whoever she feels like it. Not only is Oskar's acquiescence condoned, but the film's scenarists have no guilt feelings about promoting the idea that it's good for a 12 year old to take the law into his own hands. Even worse is the idea that it's okay for a boy who's been bullied, to take revenge, acting just as sadistically as his tormentors did toward him (Recall how Oskar takes delight in whipping the school bully across the face with a pole).<br /><br />I suppose the reason why Eli is supposed to get a free pass here is because somehow she can't help herself (after all, she got bitten back in the Middle Ages). The moral of the story is that you can commit all the horrific murders you want but as long as you counsel a 12 year old boy as to the proper etiquette in dealing with a group of stick figure, cardboard cut out bullies, then you can continue traipsing around Scandinavia, committing additional atrocious crimes.<br /><br />As for those of you still looking for true scenes of horror, 'The Right One' offers only slim pickings. In addition to the aforementioned opening scene where Hakan does his blood draining bit, you also get (hold on to yourseats!): Eli (channeling Spiderman) scurrying up the side of a hospital building, Hakan disfiguring himself by pouring hydrochloric acid over his face, in order to escape being identified by the police and Eli returning to rip off the heads and arms of the school bullies who are about to make mince meat of Oskar at the school swimming pool.<br /><br />Nothing really makes sense in 'Let the Right Thing In' including the blood sucking vampires and morally challenged 12 year olds. One can't really blame John Lindqvist, author of the book the film was based on, as well as its screenwriter, for the ultimate successful promotion of this claptrap. That honor goes to the film critics, always seeking their next great 'work of art' along with the teenage audience that went to the movie theaters in droves and (unfortunately) bought all those tickets.hospital building, Hakan disfiguring himself by pouring hydrochloric acid over his face, in order to escape being identified by the police and Eli returning to rip off the heads and arms of the school bullies who are about to make mince meat of Oskar at the school swimming pool. B004SGWYW4|4.0|By now we're all well aware of and are probably active participants in the media zombie pandemic.  What began with classic horror films has spawned countless novels, comics, television, comedies, video games, and everything else you can see or hear, infecting pop culture with the undead virus.  But I have been wondering something for quite some time now:  when will anime represent for the walking dead?  Well, that question has been answered, fellow animation fanatics.  Highschool of the Dead is here and delivering the thrills of classic Romero and the coolness of The Walking Dead, while utilizing the full spectrum of the anime genre to make this zombie apocalypse stand out as it's own animal.  And it is so close to perfectly awesome it hurts.<br /><br />Before I whine about the show's failing, let me fill you in on the good.  There's lots of it.  Right from the get-go Highschool of the Dead gets your heart racing with pure horror and some of the coolest action sequences ever animated.  As the sudden invasion quickly spreads through the halls of the high school, we are introduced to the individuals who will become our heroes.  While most students panic and quickly become prey to the ever-increasing host of ghouls, a few individuals pull it together and improvise means to fight back.  The otaku gun nut.  The kendo princess.  The ordinary kid whose best is brought out by extraordinary circumstances.  The haughty intellectual.  The airheaded school nurse. Over the first two episodes, these people will coalesce into a force to be reckoned with.  The show does an admirable job of remaining character-based and developing the relationships between it's survivors well after the extremely hectic initial outbreak, and as far as opening salvos go in survival horror, this show takes the damn cake for sure.  The art and animation are top-notch, the characters are instantly memorable, and also it's zombies!  And anime!  Sugoi, neh?<br /><br />Okay, time for some of that old-time tough love.  This show is one of the most exciting and cool anime series I've come across in a good while, and it ticks almost all of my boxes for a great zombie story, but on one front it really blows it. Rant mode: engaged.  I'm writing this paragraph as an introduction speech to present Highschool of the Dead with the first annual Najica Blitz Tactics Award for Achievement in Moronic Fanservice.  Hold your applause.  This is not a good thing.  God, this show could even teach Najica a thing or two.  It's frankly embarrassing.  I'm a big fan of sexuality in entertainment, but only when it is portrayed in a realistic and adult manner or at least matches the tone of the story.  But in a show where high school students are seeing their teachers and classmates being torn apart only to rise again and attack them in turn, having a character with ridiculously enormous breasts that make boingy noises and fly all over the place independently of each other every time she so much as blinks around is not only distracting, it literally brings the entire show down a notch.  I can deal with the pantyshots in every scene, but the incessant focus on the female characters' boobs makes this show infuriating.  At one point we get a bullet's-eye-view shot as one girl's breasts use a scissor motion to dodge said bullet.  And the bullet was fired from a rifle using another girl's breasts as a bipod (they slosh in slow motion with each shot fired).  Jesus God, WHY?!  Everything else about this show is so good!  The immaturity shown in HotD's use of fanservice is probably the most atrocious thing I've ever seen in terms of bringing down an otherwise amazing production.  Honestly, I'd be tempted to knock off another half star or so from the rating if the first season's second half didn't tone it down so much.  Be warned.<br /><br />[sighs] Glad to get that off my chest.  Okay, the good of HotD far outweighs the idiotic breast and panty fixation in an otherwise great show.  In fact, this would be instantly pushing for a spot on my favorite anime ever list if the childish sexuality had been presented in a more mature manner or omitted altogether.  If you love zombies and anime, this is a must-see.  This is brought to us by the same man who gave us the Death Note and Black Lagoon animes, after all. Each ep has a post-credits sequence that is often important to the story which makes the show feel even more theatrical than it already is and I like that the ending theme changes as well.  A mere 12 episodes is far too short a season for a show like this, and once you see the nod to Romero that is the final shot of the first season, I suspect you will be as excited as I am for the next chapter in the saga.  If they keep the fanservice manageable from here on out, we may just have a classic on our hands.ng for a spot on my favorite anime ever list if the childish sexuality had been presented in a more mature manner or omitted altogether. 0670022411|1.0|Discovery of Witches got the kind of publicity and artwork most authors of urban fantasy probably dream about, but then return to reality and taper their expectations back down.  Sadly, all of that publicity and the gorgeous artwork are nothing but a sham, a disguise for what is ultimately a poorly plotted paranormal romance that even falls through on delivering the romance.  I think even the lamest of Harlequin romances probably does a better job of showing what a budding romance looks like than Discovery of Witches managed, and Harlequin does it in a matter of 20-30 pages rather than Discovery's 582 pages.    So, here are my biggest complaints about A Discovery of Witches.  Discovery is purportedly about the mysterious magical book called Ashmole 782, and the incredibly powerful witch who inadvertently finds this work, which then causes an uproar in the supernatural community.  The inside cover teaser mentions vampires, daemons, and witches all while implying that the story is primarily about solving the mystery of Ashmole 782 and its appearance in the hands of Diana Bishop, our powerful witchy heroine.  In reality, Discovery is a weak example of paranormal romance between the witch, Diana Bishop, and a 1500 year old vampire scientist, Crusader, and hero who happened to be working nearby when Diana finds Ashmole 782.  Sadly, as an incredibly powerful witch and our heroine, Diana's character is way short of the mark.  In many parts of the book, what we are \\"told\\" in backstory about Diana's powers and her independent personality -- does not match up once the action is going.  Instead, when Harkness \\"shows\\" us Diana as a character in action, she is utterly dependent on Matthew for protection, and is in such complete denial of her awe-inspiring magic powers that she refused to learn even the most basic means of witchy self defense.  Matthew comes to her rescue over and over again, and Diana proves herself to be a helpless, clueless little woman who needs the protection of the big, bad ancient vampire.  A vampire who is so old-fashioned, and so determined to protect her from everything (her enemies, her own idiotic helplessness) that he refuses to consummate their marriage.  Sadly, these two characters are not worth 600 pages.  Neither of them demonstrates the kind of passion, and joie de vivre, that would have made a story only about their romance worthwhile reading.  When you put two mediocre characters into a poorly plotted, wannabe mystery, in the end no one is satisfied.    The saddest thing about A Discovery of Witches is that this story could have been so much more.  The potential and the intriguing premise surrounding the mysterious Ashmole 782 is real.  But in the end, Harkness failed to deliver on that potential, and in trying to make her book be a romance, and a mystery, with historical elements, and supernatural elements; she failed to achieve even one of those.  My biggest disappointment in the book is that with so much publicity, and obviously so much money funneled into making Discovery a success -- the author did not put the time or effort required into making sure her story actually lived up to all the hype.  Either the publisher or editors should have given her more time, or someone should have given her more guidance on how to effectively create a romance within a mystery without ending up with nothing but watered down, ineffective versions of both.

B004FGMV0W|3.0|Avril Lavigne's fourth studio effort, [[ASIN:B004FGMV0W Goodbye Lullaby]], is the singer's greatest departure up until this point.  On Lullaby, Lavigne sheds much of her `bratty,' `punkier' side in favor of a more serious, melancholy, `divorced' theme album.  There are worthwhile moments, but overall, Goodbye Lullaby is more of a `bore' than a genuinely enthralling effort.  Even since it has been edited to be approved by Lavigne's label, it still feels that some more appropriate, better conceived songs may have tightened this effort more. With a host of producers including Lavigne's victim of choice, ex-husband Deryck Whibley (Sum 41), the album still fails to achieve the fun of 2007`s [[ASIN:B000NA1OXY The Best Damn Thing]] or better yet, her lauded debut, [[ASIN:B000066NW0 Let Go]].<br /><br />After an off-putting intro like cut, \\"Black Star,\\" Lavigne smartly starts the album off right with the Max Martin & Shellback assisted first single, \\"What The Hell.\\" The album's best cut arguably, \\"What the Hell\\" seeks to capture the \\"Girlfriend\\" (#1 hit off of her last album) demographic.  While \\"Girlfriend\\" is most likely to be sealed in the pop history books as the more memorable cut, \\"What the Hell\\" is fun enough. \\"Push,\\" produced by Deryck Whibley is the about the best showing from Lavigne's ex where he controls the board.  The rhythmic guitars are a nice touch, but the verses, particularly the first, are a bit too wordy.  While the chorus tries to atone for Lavigne's motormouthed antics, it just doesn't quite captivate as much as, well, \\"What the Hell.\\"<br /><br />\\"Wish You Were Here\\" is another victorious moment, in the same vein as \\"What The Hell.\\" Why?  Max Martin and Shellback are behind the boards, the songwriting is `bratty' and `immature' and it highlights the Avril that fans have come to respect (if that's the right word) and love.  The vocal production surrounding Lavigne's vocals are perfect, highlighting her whiny timbre.  \\"Smile,\\" also contributed by Martin and Shellback is enjoyable, even if Lavigne may slightly grow overwrought with multiple expletives within the same line.  Where production is concerned, \\"Smile\\" ranks highly on the `totem pole.'<br /><br />\\"Stop Standing There\\" brings in Butch Walker producing, though Avril holds down the songwriting duties on her own.  It's not bad, but marks the first true shade of inconsistency that rears its ugly head upon this effort.  \\"I Love You\\" brings back the `dream team' of Lavigne, Martin & Shellback working in tandem with one another, but the airy nature of the cut just doesn't quite suit Lavigne.  \\"Everybody Hurts\\" is ok, but by this point, Whibley's rhythmic guitar texture has grown to archetypical to live an air of mysteriousness about his productions.  \\"Not Enough,\\" another Whibley production (written by Lavigne and Evan Taubenfield) is merely average, with the redeeming quality being the layers of vocals that cap off the end.<br /><br />\\"4 Real,\\" which is all Avril - songwriting and production - is a better cut, though again, the light vibe of Avril is just so out-of-place it hurts the cut.  \\"Darlin,\\" another cut by Avril, finds her speeding up the tempo slightly, but still sounds only `second rate' to her best performances and career high watermarks.  \\"Remember When\\" is bland, while \\"Goodbye\\" is pretty, but unmemorable.  The Hidden cut \\"Alice\\" brings Butch Walker back on the boards, but doesn't save the second half of the album from falling into the abyss of the sheer unexceptional.<br /><br />[[ASIN:B004FGMV0W Goodbye Lullaby]] is a nice attempt by Lavigne to make a serious, break-up oriented effort that so many artists go for whether it be Marvin Gaye, Usher, Pink, etc.  The problem is, Lavigne compromises herself as the artists that fans have come to know and love and this more `somber' side is less convincing and vivacious. B000002KY8|4.0|By the time that Prince released 1999 in 1982, he was already a music-industry veteran with four albums to his name, one of them a straight-up classic. Coming on the heels of the slick and relatively successful Prince (1979), the stripped-down, New Wave-influenced, and lyrically filthy Dirty Mind (1980) had served notice that Prince was more than just another R&B funkmeister and/or balladeer while simultaneously horrifying his record company and momentarily stopping his building commercial momentum cold. Said momentum was restarted handily, however, by 1999. For most in suburban white-bread America, this is where the Prince story really begins, the story of a musician who would dominate pop music for the next decade.    And that's kind of funny, because 1999 is hardly a conventional pop album. It's a double, first of all. And there are just eleven songs spread over its four sides, many of them extended workouts stretching toward the ten-minute mark. When we look a little closer, what we see here is Prince learning to balance his artistic ambitions with commercial considerations, something he became almost uniquely gifted at. And so he delivers the pop on the first side, in the form of the unstoppable (if now dated) party jam \\"1999,\\" the melodic funk of the album's monster hit \\"Little Red Corvette,\\" and the perky and too often forgotten little gem \\"Delirious.\\" With that dispensed with, Prince feels free to get a bit weird over the next three sides.    Whether it's a case of overexposure or something else, for me that's where 1999 really begins. Songs as long as most of these are just crying out for pat rock-critic responses -- \\"could have used some editing,\\" \\"this would have made a terrific single album with a little judicious pruning,\\" etc. -- but I'm not going to indulge. As someone who grew up listening to Yes and Pink Floyd, I rather like long songs, if they're doing something interesting. And these epics are consistently interesting, chock full of sudden change-ups, instrumental breakdowns, and groove, groove, groove. I wouldn't have \\"D.M.S.R.\\" (8.17), \\"Automatic\\" (9.28), or \\"Lady Cab Driver\\" (8.19) one second shorter. Unlike so many dance and electronica acts -- and make no mistake, 80% of the instruments on this album come equipped with a microprocessor -- Prince knows how to construct long songs that actually go someplace, rather than repeating and juxtaposing a handful of fragments again and again.    Lyrically... well, this is a pre-Jehovah's Witness Prince album, so you know what you're in for. I have to say, though, that what seemed excitingly transgressive on Dirty Mind is already growing stale here. It's not so much the \\"suggestive\\" content (where would music be without sex?) as the really direct stuff that irritates me. When Prince says he wants to \\"F the taste out of your mouth\\" in \\"Let's Pretend We're Married,\\" for instance, it doesn't sound exciting or sexy at all. It just sounds like a little boy with a potty mouth; one feels Prince should be outgrowing this sort of thing by now. There's also an element of dominance and submission, even of rape fantasy, to some of these tracks. This is most pronounced on \\"Lady Cab Driver,\\" in which Prince -- sorry, there's no better way to put this -- hate-Fs a woman, while we hear her screams of either ecstasy or pain -- we aren't quite sure which. Call me a prude, but I have to admit that I find this somewhere between embarrassing and genuinely disturbing when I play the album today. I like to think that some of it is played for laughs, and, indeed, a song like \\"International Lover\\" is so over the top that it's impossible to take it seriously as a seduction ballad. In a stirring victory of delusions of grandeur over reality, Prince, 5'2 tall and 115 pounds soaking wet, tells his would-be lover how lucky she is that \\"this body [his] is free tonight,\\" and then proceeds to make increasingly strained metaphors between sex and air travel for the next six minutes or so.    Moreinteresting to a digital historian like me is Prince's obsession with computer technology, not only in his instrumentation but lyrically in tracks like \\"Something in the Water (Does Not Compute).\\" Computers would continue to be a theme not only in Prince's work but in the work of many other musicians from this period: Kraftwerk's Computer World, Laurie Anderson's \\"O, Superman,\\" etc. It's an instructive reminder that, before the Internet revolution of the mid-1990s, there was another computer revolution, as the little machines were suddenly everywhere: in homes, in arcades, and of course in music, and that they were therefore on lots of people's minds.    While mostly performed on synthesizers and drum machines, Prince's one-man band does include occasional guitar. Prince has always ladled out his guitar leads very sparingly, a move that, whether done purposefully or not, has probably done a lot to enhance his reputation as a guitar god. (If he only delivers one or two solos per album, it's hard to see how limited his bag of tricks really is.) This album is no exception to that, with only \\"Automatic\\" and especially \\"All the Critics Love You in New York\\" containing prominent leads, although several others contain tasty examples of Prince's ironically underrated rhythm and bass playing. \\"New York\\" is a particular delight, with Prince carving out shards of noise from his guitar over thumping electronics and half-spoken lyrics that are for once about neither sex nor computers, but rather about the pretentiousness of the New York music scene. It reminds me of the best track Yoko Ono ever cut, \\"Walking on Thin Ice,\\" anchored as that track is by the most interesting guitar parts John Lennon ever played.    \\"New York\\" would have been unimaginable on an earlier Prince album. Indeed, the whole of 1999 shows an already accomplished artist dramatically expanding his range. That's in fact literally true -- after singing on earlier albums virtually exclusively in falsetto, Princesuddenly shifts to his normal register most of the time here, while always keeping that gorgeous falsetto in reserve for the times when he really needs it. One could argue that Prince's refusal to grow emotionally as he grew musically ultimately keep him from joining the pantheon of the greatest of the great. That musical growth is so pronounced on this album, though, that Prince's limitations can be easy to overlook, at least while it's playing.

0316044938|5.0|The Lovely Bones is a rare and riveting novel uniquely told in the perspective of a dead girl as she and her family must cope with the tragedy of her death. Susie Salmon was an average fourteen year old with your superficially stereotypical family living in the standard suburban neighborhood of the 70's. Within this everyday setting, Alice Sebold unearthed one of the \\"unthinkables\\" in that type of modern society and community. Through Susie's perspective, readers see the insides and outs of those faced in this type of heartbreaking circumstance. Susie is forced to accept the outcome of her murder as she resides in heaven, looking over the people she left on earth. Her parents' relationship is rocked, and each family member and friends are forced to deal with her untimely murder. Susie's life is ripped away from her too soon, and she never gets the chance to fully grow up; from heaven she watches her sister Lindsey grow up and experience a life she never got the chance to live. Susie struggles to reveal her killer and comfort her loved ones. Meanwhile, her father is tenaciously driven to catch his daughter's murderer and avenge her death. Another shocking truth about the suburban neighborhood is revealed, as Mr. Harvey, the strange man next door, turns out to be a serial killer who made Susie his latest victim in an unsuspecting environment. The community does not want to accept that a deranged serial killer was in their midst living close by, although many conclusions are drawn. The events of her death bring about many connections and represented \\"merely bones of a body that would become whole at some unpredictable time in the near future.\\"  This novel is told in a very down-to-earth tone, exposing one of the tragedies in the American heartland. Sebold's writing style is straightforward, emotional, sentimental, reflective, and optimistic. The plot loses momentum towards the end, but all in all, this is a great read that will keep you reading into the dark hours of the night as you read on to find out what will happen next.

B004FRX0MY|3.0|I love the concept of a cloud drive, especially from my preferred vendor of MP3s, however the player/store needs a reworking. There are some performance issues which I'm sure they're working on since this is the initial launch, however my real gripes are with their overall approach to playing music from a their own remote source.    Why must shopping/playing from cloud/playing locally feel like three completely separate tasks in one app? Instead of trying to jam two apps into one, and still giving the user that segregated feeling, give this app some synergy! I know that's pretty lame buzzword, but listen...    The concept is simple at the high level:  The user's list of tracks should be unification of what is online only, and what they have locally... NOT two separate lists. I'd like to see indicators of a track's source, but I should be able to make a playlist (or at least a track selection) that pulls from BOTH sources. This would actually save their bandwidth to pull from local storage whenever possible. Keep the ability to switch to \\"offline\\" mode, but it should not be the default, and it should NOT feel like a separate app/task/list. Much better user experience.     On top of that, Amazon could sell much more music through the player by giving us the ability to sample music similar to our own library, or what we just listened to. They've got their \\"similar customers\\" logic down pretty good, why not put it right in the player? Don't force it on us, but offer it. As much flack as Apple gets for it's \\"Genius\\" lists, that stuff sells a lot of music. I would think this would be easy for Amazon.    My last suggestion may be a pipe dream but for Android 2.2+ users, Amazon really should tie into the Voice Actions events. When I say \\"Listen to Tool\\" (or better yet, music I don't have) to my phone, the only apps on my phone that support that action are Pandora and Last.fm... Why not Amazon? If it's something I have, play it! If not, give me samples with the ability to quickly purchase. I think that could be a HUGE driver of sales.    THINK ABOUT IT DEVELOPERS, THINK ABOUT IT!    Lastly, as many other reviewers have said, app functionality dedicated solely to browsing for mp3's should be separate... or built into one of your other shopping apps, not into the player. I know you think that's gonna help sell music, but now I have to click extra times to get to the market because I normally use this app as a player, so I normally just wait till I'm at a computer, and sometimes I forget what I wanted to look for. Not an optimal experience for the user (or the salesman)!

1579128149|4.0|Theodore Gray loathes incandescent lightbulbs. With a passion.<br /><br />Fortunately for us, he loves elements. The avid element collector and founder of Wolfram Research shares his affinity for the 118+ substances that comprise the periodic table, and in the process gives us a fascinating, popular overview of the subject. If you can ignore some of the less-than-stellar writing and pontificating about subjects unrelated to the elements, such as the incandescent lightbulb screeds, you'll love this book.<br /><br />The first thing that grabs readers of _The Elements_ is the superlative photography and graphic design. This book is total eye candy. Most of the photos are from the author's collection, and the uniformity of the excellence can't be denied. The design is perfect as well. Even-numbered pages introduce each element with a page-filling depiction and the odd contain text and supporting images of items that consist of the element of note.<br /><br />In addition, Gray and his designers have done a masterful job including the more dry numerical facts about each element in a convenient sidebar on odd pages that mixes pure numbers with easy to comprehend graphs and charts of states of matter temperatures, spectrography, crystallography, and electron shell positions. That succinct, visual consistency would draw raves from Edward Tufte.<br /><br />The author's love of collecting the elements themselves is clear from the text. He adores his subject matter and this comes through in each page. Gray shares not only his passion, but also interesting facts about each element.<br /><br />But the text is the Achilles heel of _The Elements_. In reality, there's not much of it. Often what is there reads as if it came from a _USA Today_ article rather than a science book. In some cases, how Gray feels about an element dominates any facts about it, making the book less than authoritative for the scientifically inclined. _The Elements_ is clearly aimed at the casual reader--and sometimes it is too casual even for them. And then there's the incandescent lightbulb rants...<br /><br />One niggling complaint is that the black pages, while they do an excellent job of providing a background for the superb photography, also show fingerprints like mad. Pages end up splotched with shiny black on regular black, which catches one's eye as much as the excellent photos. A shame.<br /><br />If ever there was a perfect coffee table science book, this would be it. Lovely to look at and filled with just enough text to fulfill its coffee table aspirations, it makes a fine addition to anyone's library, scientist or not.And then there's the incandescent lightbulb rants... 0061134007|5.0|During World War II, 300,000 German troops occupied Norway. The royal family fled Norway and remained in exile throughout the war. Many Norwegians supported German rule; there was the infamous collaborationist government of Vidkun Quisling.  An estimated fifteen thousand Norwegians joined or were forced into the German army and fought alongside German troops. Some forty thousand armed Norwegians resisted German occupation.    After the war, the resistors were glorified; those who supported the Germans were vilified, with many prosecuted by the post-war government. This novel by Jo Nesbo largely concerns a Norwegian who served in the German army.  Much of the book is set in 1999-2000 when he's in his seventies and is dying of cancer. He's very bitter about how he and others like him were treated after the war. He experienced brutal combat against the Soviets on the Eastern Front and feels he was fighting to defend Norway from Soviet occupation.     Oslo police detective Harry Hole is involved in the investigation of neo-Nazi activity and a possible assassination plot during the dawn of the new century. His investigation leads him to events half a century earlier in the 1940's.     The shift from the forties to the late nineties is somewhat confusing. There are also some confusing identity changes. But it is all written very well. I found that when I had to put the book down due to various commitments, I remained interested enough to return and soon again became absorbed in the story.     Hole seems similar to other Scandinavian detectives I have encountered in fiction land. Like Henning Mankell's  Kurt Wallender (Sweden) and Arnaldur Indridason's Erlender Sveinnson (Iceland), Hole is viewed  with little respect by society as something like a low/mid level bureaucrat who packs a gun. B000TGCR38|4.0|I saw Paul Verhoeven's Black Book in the theatre(not to be confused with Little Black Book, a horrendous romantic comedy.) Paul Verhoeven is a director whose American portfolio includes such films as Basic Instinct, Starship Troopers, Robocop, Total Recall, and the truly horrible Showgirls. None of these films are particularly good, and all seem to share a common theme of excessive violence, of excessive sex, or a combination of both.    I was astonished to learn that Verhoeven has directed two movies that are considered the greatest Dutch films of the twentieth century: Soldier of Orange, and Turkish Delight. While the plot synopsis of Turkish Delight does not appeal to me, Soldier of Orange, the story of students involved in the Dutch resistance, does. I hope to see that movie some day.    Black Book was of particular interest to me because it deals with the Dutch Resistance during World War II. Being of Dutch extraction, and being a student of history, I was excited about seeing this movie. My excitement was tempered by a certain anxiousness. What would Verhoeven do? Would there be any resemblance of actual historical events, or would it be an excuse for bloody shoot-outs and mindless sex?  In the end, I was neither satisfied nor disappointed. The movie begins with the words \\"based on actual events\\" which, in the film world, is code for \\"we dressed everybody old-fashioned and then made up a story.\\" The movie does a fine job in recreating the war-time Netherlands (I think), but the fictional characters do not correspond to any Dutch reality. The film is poignant at times, while at other times it is so over the top it borders on ridiculous. There is a lot of violence, which is to be expected in a movie dealing with a clash between the resistance and the Nazis. There is also a lot of nudity, which is, for the most part, completely unnecessary.    Carrice Van Houten plays Rachel Stein, a Jewish woman in hiding. Circumstances force her out of hiding and into the resistance. Further events lead her to befriend the German SD officer, Ludwig Mntze whom she initially uses for information but later falls in love with. I had a difficult time with the idea that a Dutch Jew could have possibly fallen for any Nazi, no matter how sympathetic, during this dark period of history. I myself could not build up any positive feeling towards Mntze, a man who presumably would have served Nazism to the end if it had been successful.  Perhaps this is just due to my own bias, however. The film follows Rachel Stein's trials and tribulations right through to the liberation of the Netherlands.    Despite the problems I had with this movie, I still consider it to be very well-done. While it often seemed forced to me, it packs an emotional punch. The dark underbelly of the resistance is shown in all of its ugly glory. Van Houten's performance is superb, the story is intense, and many of the characters are intriguing. Verhoeven's often heavy hand succeeds in sculpting a work of true merit.

B0000033OT|5.0|After I purchased this the other night I listened to it on the car ride home. Musically it was such an experience to absorb that I actually had to give it a second listen before I could fully comprehend what I was hearing. Over the years I've heard about what a classic this album was and sort of ignored it because that word is thrown about far too much for it's own good as we all know. So after listening to it that second time I have to say the word classic flat out isn't enough to describe this album. It truely is the work of someone whose musical mind was,isn't and probably isn't to fully be understood. A Howard University graduate Hathaway had been working up to this album since the mid 60's and featuring a band consisting of some of Chi-Towns best musicians in soul and jazz such as Phil Upchurch,Louis Satterfield and sax players King Curtis and future EWF mainstay Don Myrick this album managed to create something incredible and often mind bending.<br /><br />  Years before the term neo soul was ever coined Donny Hathaway was on the forefront of all of it. Having absorbed just about every genre the city had to offer in the day from jazz,R&B,gospel,blues and the newer subgenre of funk Donny decided to explore all of it at the same time in songs that really baught to mind the musical and lyrical themes are emotional release and expression that all these genres shared and how they were connected. So therefore the album could not be more appropriately titled. The music is abound with some intense,revered lines on Donny's trademark Fender Rhodes electric piano and rhythms of all different sides. \\"Voices Inside (Everything Is Everything)\\" and \\"Sugar Lee\\" are both raw,bluesy late 60's style jazz-funk also featuring the musicians talking in the backround. He also provides three cover songs. Ray Charles' \\"I Believe To My Soul\\" really draws out the songs heavy gospel/blues hybrid while Errol Garner's \\"Misty\\" and Nina Simone's \\"To Be Young,Gifted And Black\\" all receive appropriately jazzy treatments.<br /><br />  \\"Je Vous Amie\\" and \\"Thank You Master (For My Soul)\\" are pure Atlantic style gospel soul ballads somewhat in the vein of Aretha. On the latter his distressed mental state does come out to some degree but as a message to the people;while singing of his faith he also adds that \\"the walls of my room are not the walls of my grave\\",a statement that impacted heavily on me in particular in it's statement of independence from the demands of the world around him and how people with a different way of thinking can be ignored and misunderstood. Now \\"Tryin' Times\\" and \\"The Ghetto\\" are a different matter altogether. On the former we have a bluesy R&B stomper more blatantly illustrating the social ills of the time in a more classic blues tradition and on the latin infused funky soul of \\"The Ghetto\\" you basically hear sounds-people taking,babies crying and that's all it really needs to say. The bonus track on this album is...well if the proceeding tracks were incredible this one almost defies description. \\"A Dream\\",an cinamatic,almost operation jazzy soul piece with really no tempo at all it features Hathaway on some incredible scaling vocals and cosmically poetic lyrical imagry. So it's true what everyone said:people both in and outside of the R&B community should've paid more attention to this man's outreaching approch when he was around. But they have been in more recent years so...better late than never.nd Nina Simone's \\"To Be Young,Gifted And Black\\" all receive appropriately jazzy treatments.                                            \\"Je Vous Amie\\" and \\"Thank You Master (For My Soul)\\" are pure Atlantic style gospel soul ballads somewhat in the vein of Aretha. 006112429X|4.0|I'd read that this book was pretty good and as someone with an interest in school shootings - I was present when my alma mater had one - I didn't need to be sold on checking this book out. This review contains spoilers. Seriously. I spoil the ending.    This must be noted immediately, as it is a problem apparent right from the start and never lets up: Shriver's vocabulary is incredibly pretentious. I don't know why her editor allowed so many uselessly huge words to litter the text, but nobody saw fit to change this. Unless the impractically large words were a literary device to illustrate how obnoxious the narrator is, there was no need for this.    I have read reviews that indicate the reader did not like the book specifically because the protagonist, Eva, was unlikeable. I don't think that's fair. Just because you don't like the character, it doesn't automatically mean she was poorly developed or unrealistic. I didn't too much love Eva, but I don't think I needed to. Her underlying motivation is to decide whether or not she is responsible for her son Kevin's murderous rampage and whether you like her or not is irrelevant in my opinion. Eva is a supercilious glutton for punishment who makes poor choices regarding what's best for her family, her marriage, herself, and her children. It's sort of an ironic parody of the typical self-absorbed, self-satisfied wealthy white woman. It is unclear whether Shriver's true goal is to make you hate or sympathize with Eva.     The recurring focus on other school shootings feels like a misstep. It feels contrived for the purposes of the plot (some of the incidents seem fictional while others, like Columbine, obviously are not) and takes away from the climax of Kevin's shooting. She over-saturated the story with them.    Shriver is asking whether nature or nurture is responsible for the monster that is Kevin. My biggest issue with this book is that this entire premise, which the novel hinges itself on, is irrelevant. It doesn't work because right from the start it is plainly apparent that Kevin is a horrible little a-hole and nothing could have fixed that. The problem is that Kevin, and the other child Celia, are presented from birth with unrealistically preordained personalities. There is no room for nurture to have a hand in the way Eva's children turn out because from birth they have such exaggeratedly pronounced personality traits. While theoretically it may be possible that Eva is an unreliable narrator and the truth was that her reactions to Kevin shaped him from birth, the tertiary characters disprove this - even as an infant Kevin was perceived by everyone around him (nannies, baby sitters, neighbors, teachers, classmates) as unpleasant and disturbed. You can't ask whether a child could have turned out differently when you make it clear from the start that the child has always had these traits and nothing anyone did to intervene changed them. Part of this flaw is probably due to the fact that Shriver herself has no children. I don't either, but I have still observed enough to know that children don't have fixed personalities this way, especially at such a young age.    Kevin's evil nature is extremely pronounced and unavoidable. When he unexpectedly begins to speak at age three - in full sentences - I half expected him to laugh maniacally, exposing pointed white teeth and rolling around matching white eyes, and run madly around the room with demonic speed and strength. It just doesn't make sense to base the entire theme around the question of nature or nurture when the character is inescapably depicted as a bad seed. There is no ambiguity.    While some view the character of Celia as a deliberately manipulative means to pull the heartstrings, I enjoyed her function in the plot. It is heart wrenching to think of such a sweet, harmless little girl being hurt. And despite Eva's constant expressions of love for her husband Franklin, he is so stubbornly deluded about Kevin that you almostwant him to suffer his comeuppance. I definitely did not see the \\"twist\\" at the end, and while I do think the previous language describing them IS manipulative in order to conceal this twist, I think it works.      Apart from these criticisms, this reveal is one of the most (if not THE most) redeeming factors about the book because it forces you to question everything before it and allows for many more interpretations about Kevin's evilness. There is little question what a monster Kevin is when you know the full extent of what he did, seemingly just to leave Eva alive to suffer. It also suggests that Kevin's motive for the shooting is simply that he knows his mother doesn't love him (so as extra punishment, he kills the people she DOES love) and he's taken the petulance of \\"acting out\\" too far. This extra step of cruelty vindicates the feelings Eva has had all along and makes the big question of \\"why\\" a lot simpler - Kevin's just a hateful, murderous a-hole. There is no big philosophical reason. Kevin is an angry, privileged little a-hole whose sense of cultural entitlement went so far that it allowed him to take the lives of others because he felt he could, the same as any other school shooter.     It also justifies some of the other problems I had, such as why Shriver chose to keep Kevin odiously, frustratingly alive when most of these kids commit suicide, or why Eva doesn't simply write the kid off to rot instead of seeking his approval/understanding/abuse/whatever. You want him to die because he is so heinous but now you think having to live with himself - who is the person he really hates the most - is the greatest punishment of all. It was simply a clever and well-done twist that successfully achieves greater depth for both Eva and Kevin. Before this I'd almost decided the novel was self indulgent and meaningless, particularly when Shriver directly allows Kevin the opportunity to explain his motives and he not only spouts a bunch of crap but Shriver cuts himoff for no reason in the story because as the writer she was out of stupid excuses and didn't really know what she was trying to say. But I think making Kevin not only a school shooter but a kid who murdered his loving father and sister was a great reveal allowing for more thought on human nature instead of on niche psychos.     While I'd have preferred more punishment for Kevin in the end, there was really no way for this story to have a happy ending, and as such is life. Shriver told a harrowing tale and whether you love it or hate it, it's one that will stick with you.
